The Eternal Movement Dilemma
Razi presents a dilemma proving past movements have a beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's dilemma proving that past movements cannot be eternal and must have a beginning.

Core Idea
Either a first movement exists or an eternal movement leads to absurdity — both prove a beginning.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents a logical dilemma that cuts through any attempt to posit an eternal past of events. The argument is elegant but requires careful attention to its two branches. Let's walk through each horn and see why both lead to the same conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma setup
The proof begins by stating two exhaustive possibilities: either some movement occurred in eternity (azal) or none did. Recognize that this covers all logical options.
Eternity here means beginningless time before creation.
2
Analyze the first horn
If no movement occurred in eternity, then the set of all movements must have a starting point. This directly yields a first movement, which is the desired conclusion.
3
Analyze the second horn
If some movement did occur in eternity, consider that movement. If it is not preceded by any other movement, it is itself the first — conclusion reached. If it is preceded, then eternity would be preceded by something else, which is absurd.
The absurdity is that 'eternal' would depend on something earlier, a contradiction.
4
Recognize the role of impossibility
The proof relies on the impossibility of an infinite regress: if every eternal movement is preceded, you get an infinite chain, which is impossible because then eternity itself would have a beginning.
This is a standard argument in kalam against an infinite past.
5
Connect to the conclusion
Both horns lead to the existence of a first movement. Thus, the world's past events (movements) cannot be eternal — they have a beginning.
This proof supports the existence of a Creator who initiated motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثالث : وهو أنه إما أن يقال : حصل في الأزل شيء من هذه الحركات أو لم يحصل. فإن لم يحصل في الأزل شيء من هذه الحركات والحوادث، وجب أن تكون لمجموع هذه الحركات والحوادث بداية وأول. وهو المطلوب. وإن حصل في الأزل شيء من هذه الحركات، فتلك الحركة الحاصلة في الأزل إن لم تكن مسبوقة بغيرها كانت تلك الحركة أول الحركات وهو المطلوب. وإن كانت مسبوقة بغيرها لزم أن يكون الأزل مسبوقاً بغيره. وهو محال.
The third proof: It is either said: Something of these movements occurred in eternity or it did not occur. If nothing of these movements and events occurred in eternity, then it is necessary that this totality of movements and events has a beginning and a first. That is what is sought. And if something of these movements occurred in eternity, then that movement that occurred in eternity, if it is not preceded by another, then that movement is the first of the movements—that is what is sought. And if it is preceded by another, then it would necessitate that eternity is preceded by something else. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhān) that past movements must have a finite beginning. The author presents a dilemma. Either no movement occurred in eternity (i.e., before the world), in which case there must be a first movement (since movements now exist). Or some movement did occur in eternity. In that case, consider that movement: if it is not itself preceded by any other movement, then it is the first—the desired conclusion. If it is preceded by another movement, then that earlier movement would be before the eternal movement, which would mean eternity (the beginningless time) is preceded by something, which is impossible. Thus, both horns lead to the existence of a first movement. The proof uses logical necessity and the impossibility of an infinite regress.

Key Terms
برهان
أزل
حركة
حادث
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a movement occurs in eternity, it could be preceded by an infinite chain.
Correction: Razi shows that if an eternal movement is preceded, then eternity itself would be preceded by something else, which is logically impossible. Thus an infinite regress is impossible.

Checkpoint
In Razi's third proof, if a movement occurred in eternity and is preceded by another, what does that imply?
A) That movement is the first.
B) That movement is impossible.
C) That eternity is preceded by something else, which is impossible.
D) That there must be an infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
Razi's proof shows that an infinite past of events is impossible, thus the world had a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every movement you have ever made is part of a chain that must have had a first beginning, what does that mean for your own existence? Are you comfortable with the idea that your life is not self-originating?
?
Razi's proof shows that an infinite past leads to logical absurdity. How does this truth affect the way you think about the time you have been given? Does it make you more aware of the urgency of now?
?
If the world had a beginning, then there is a Creator who chose to bring it into existence. What does that demand from you today? Is your life reflecting gratitude for that beginning?

The Fourth Proof: Astronomical Ratios
Razi uses the ratio of Saturn's to Sun's revolutions to prove finitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the ratio of Saturn's revolutions to the Sun's revolutions to prove that all celestial movements have a beginning, thereby establishing the finitude of the universe.

Core Idea
Finite ratios prove a finite past: Saturn's slower motion shows the universe had a starting point.

In this proof, Imam al-Razi draws on a simple astronomical observation: Saturn takes about 30 years to orbit the Sun, while the Sun completes one orbit per year. By comparing the numbers of revolutions, he constructs a logical argument demonstrating that the universe cannot be eternal and must have a beginning. Let's walk through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the astronomical observation
Razi states that in the duration of one revolution of Saturn, the Sun completes thirty revolutions. This is based on the observable periodicities of these celestial bodies.
In the geocentric model, Saturn has the longest orbital period among the known planets.
2
Apply the finitude principle
Since Saturn's revolutions are fewer than the Sun's, and anything that is less than something else is finite, the number of Saturn's revolutions must be finite.
This principle is a logical axiom: if A < B and B is finite, then A is finite.
3
Conclude Saturn's motion has a beginning
If the number of revolutions is finite, then the series of revolutions must have started at some point; hence Saturn's motion has a beginning.
An infinite series would have no beginning and no finite count.
4
Extend to all motions
Razi argues that since the Sun's revolutions are a finite multiple (30 times) of Saturn's finite number, they too are finite. This reasoning extends to all celestial motions, so every movement has a beginning.
The key point: a finite number multiplied by a finite number remains finite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الرابع : أن في مدة دورة واحدة من أدوار (زحل) تتحرك الشمس ثلاثين دورة. فإذن عدد دورات (زحل) تكون أقل من دورات (الشمس) وكل ما كان أقل من غيره فهو متناه. فإذن عدد دوراته متناه، فلحركة (زحل) بداية . وإذا كان كذلك، وجب أن يكون أيضًا لجميع الحركات بداية لأن ضعف المتناهي مرارًا متناهية، يكون متناهيا.
The fourth proof: In the duration of one revolution of (Saturn), the sun moves thirty revolutions. Thus, the number of revolutions of (Saturn) is less than the revolutions of (Sun). And everything that is less than something else is finite. Thus, the number of its revolutions is finite, so the movement of (Saturn) has a beginning. And if that is so, then it is necessary that also all movements have a beginning, because doubling the finite repeatedly yields a finite [result].
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This proof uses an astronomical observation: in the time it takes Saturn to complete one orbit, the Sun completes about thirty orbits (in the geocentric model). Since the number of Saturn's revolutions is less than the Sun's, and anything that is less than something else is finite, Saturn's number of revolutions must be finite. Therefore, Saturn's motion has a beginning. Then, since the Sun's revolutions are a multiple (30 times) of Saturn's finite number, and multiplying a finite number by a finite number yields a finite number, the Sun's revolutions must also be finite. Extending this reasoning, all movements must have a beginning. The argument relies on the principle that a finite multiple of a finite number remains finite.

Key Terms
دورة
زحل
شمس
متناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if Saturn's revolutions are finite, the Sun's could be infinite.
Correction: Razi shows that since the Sun's revolutions are a finite multiple (30 times) of Saturn's finite number, they must also be finite. Finite multiplied by finite remains finite.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the fourth proof?
A) The ratio of Saturn's and Sun's revolutions.
B) The possibility of infinite regression.
C) The definition of movement.
D) The nature of eternity.

Key Takeaway
Razi's fourth proof uses the finite ratio between Saturn's and the Sun's revolutions to demonstrate that all celestial motions must have a beginning, refuting the eternity of the universe.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every movement in the universe had a beginning, including the beating of your heart and the thoughts in your mind, how does that change the way you see each moment? Will you pause today to thank the One who started it all?
?
Razi used the slow cycle of Saturn to deduce finitude. What is one slow or insignificant part of your daily life that, if reflected upon, could point you to the Creator's wisdom? Will you commit to using that as a daily reminder?
?
This proof ends with the conclusion that all movements have a beginning. Your existence began at a specific moment. Knowing that, what is the ultimate purpose of your finite time here? What action will you take today to live that purpose more fully?

The Fifth Proof: Comparing Two Sets of Past Events
Razi proves the finiteness of past events via comparison of two series.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses a comparison of two collections of past events to demonstrate that the series of past events is finite, thereby proving the existence of a Beginner.

Core Idea
If one infinite set is longer than another, the shorter must be finite, proving all past events finite.

Razi's fifth proof for the existence of a Beginner uses a clever comparison between two sets of past events. By imagining two overlapping timelines, he shows that the assumption of an infinite past leads to a contradiction. This lesson walks through the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the flow of the argument. Don't try to analyze yet—just let the logic sink in.
The passage is dense; reread if needed.
2
Identify the two collections
The passage mentions two collections: Set A from today to eternity, and Set B from the time of the Flood to eternity. Notice that Set A is longer by the finite interval between the Flood and today.
3
Visualize the mental alignment
Imagine aligning the two collections at their finite ends (the present). This allows you to compare the two series as they stretch back into the past.
The Arabic word 'وهم' refers to mental estimation or imagination.
4
Consider the two possible outcomes
The proof examines two outcomes: either the shorter collection (B) does not come to an end before the longer (A)—which is impossible—or it does come to an end, meaning it is finite.
The impossibility is that 'a thing with another would be the same as without it.'
5
Reach the conclusion
Once the shorter set is finite, the longer set, which exceeds it by a finite amount, must also be finite. Thus, all past events are finite from the side of eternity.
This finiteness implies a beginning of time, pointing to a Beginner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الخامس : إذا فرضنا الحوادث الماضية من اليوم إلى الأزل جملة، ومن زمان الطوفان إلى الأزل جملة أخرى . فلا شك أن الجملة الأولى أزيد من الجملة الثانية، بما بين زمان الطوفان إلى هذا اليوم. فإذا طبقنا في الوهم الطرف المتناهي من الجملة الزائدة على الطرف المتناهي من الجملة الناقصة، حتى يقابل كل فرد من أفراد إحدى الجملتين بما يشابهه في المرتبة من الجملة الأخرى. فإن لم تنقض الجملة الناقصة عن الزائدة في الطرف الآخر، كان الشيء مع غيره كهو لا مع غيره. وهذا محال . وإن انقطعت الجملة الناقصة من ذلك الطرف، كانت متناهية من ذلك الطرف، وكانت متناهية من جانب الأزل. والزائد زاد عليها بمقدار متناه، والزائد على المتناهي بمقدار متناه يكون متناهيا . فالكل متناه في جانب الأزل.
The fifth proof: If we suppose the past events from today to eternity as one collection, and from the time of the Flood to eternity as another collection. There is no doubt that the first collection exceeds the second collection by the amount between the time of the Flood and today. Then if we mentally align the finite end of the exceeding collection with the finite end of the deficient collection, until each individual of one collection corresponds to its counterpart in rank from the other collection; then if the deficient collection does not come to an end before the exceeding collection at the other end, then the thing with another would be the same as it without another. That is impossible. And if the deficient collection comes to an end from that side, then it is finite from that side, and it is finite from the side of eternity. And the exceeding [collection] increased upon it by a finite amount, and that which exceeds the finite by a finite amount is finite. Thus, the whole is finite from the side of eternity.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This proof compares two sets of past events: Set A from today back to eternity, and Set B from a specific past event (the Flood) back to eternity. Set A is longer by the finite interval between the Flood and today. By mentally aligning the two sets at the present end, we see that the shorter set (B) will run out at the eternal end before the longer set (A). If both sets were infinite, they would be equal and one would not run out. But B does run out, so it is finite at the eternal end. Then adding the finite gap from Flood to today gives a finite total for Set A. Therefore, all past events are finite in number. This argument assumes that an infinite set cannot be exceeded by another set.

Key Terms
جملة
وهم
طرف
متناهي
طوفان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two series both go back infinitely, one can be longer than the other.
Correction: Razi shows that if one is longer by a finite amount, the shorter cannot be infinite; it must have a beginning.

Checkpoint
What does the fifth proof use to show that past events are finite?
A) The impossibility of an actual infinite.
B) The comparison of two different collections of past events.
C) The definition of eternity.
D) The fact that the Flood occurred.

Key Takeaway
By comparing two overlapping sets of past events, Razi demonstrates that the past must be finite, leading to the proof of a Beginner.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If all past events are finite, that includes every moment of your life. Your existence has a beginning. Before that, you were nothing. How does this realization affect your understanding of your own importance and your dependence on the Creator? What will you do today to acknowledge that you come from nothing and are accountable?
?
Razi's proof shows that time itself is finite in the past. This means you are not a product of an eternal, meaningless chain. You were intentionally brought into being. If your entire life is a finite gift from the Eternal, how will you spend the finite moments you have left differently starting now?
?
The proof argues that if the past were infinite, absurdities would follow. Do you live as if this life is all there is, or as if it is a finite prelude to eternity? If the latter, what is one habit you will change today to align your life with the reality that your existence is finite and purposeful?

The Sixth Proof: Infinite Past Would Prevent Today
Razi argues that today's existence proves past cycles are finite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi's sixth proof uses the existence of today to demonstrate that past cycles must be finite, thereby refuting the eternity of motion.

Core Idea
If the past were infinite, today could never arrive.

Razi presents a powerful reductio ad absurdum: if past cycles of motion were infinite, then for today to occur, an infinite series would need to be completed—an impossibility. Yet here we are, today exists. This simple logical deduction forces the conclusion that the past is finite, and therefore motion had a beginning. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Let the logical flow become clear before you analyse.
Notice the concise, syllogistic style of the proof.
2
Identify the conditional structure
The proof uses an 'if...then' structure. Identify the premise, the conditional, the impossibility, and the conclusion based on the actual existence of today.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why does this proof refute the idea of eternal motion? How does it link to proving a Creator?
Connects to the broader topic of divine existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان السادس : لو كانت الأدوار الماضية غير متناهية، لكان حدوث اليوم موقوفا على انقضاء ما لا نهاية له وانقضاء ما لا نهاية له ،محال فيلزم أن يكون حدوث اليوم موقوفًا على شرط محال، والموقوف على الشرط المحال، لا يوجد. فكان يلزم أن لا يوجد اليوم وحيث وجد علمنا أن الأمور المنقضية قبل هذا اليوم متناهية، فثبت بهذه البراهين أن القول يكون الأجسام متحركة في الأزل محال.
The sixth proof: If the past cycles were infinite, then the occurrence of today would depend upon the completion of what has no end. And the completion of what has no end is impossible. Thus, it would follow that the occurrence of today depends upon an impossible condition, and that which depends upon an impossible condition does not exist. Consequently, today would not exist. But today does exist, so we know that the things that elapsed before today are finite. Thus, it is established by these proofs that the claim that bodies are moving in eternity is impossible.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This is a reductio ad absurdum. If past cycles (e.g., celestial rotations) were infinite, then for today to occur, an infinite number of cycles must have already been completed. But completing an infinite series is impossible. Therefore, today's occurrence would be impossible. Since today clearly exists, the cycles elapsed before today must be finite. Consequently, bodies cannot have been in motion eternally; there must have been a beginning of motion. This proof directly links the present moment to the finitude of the past.

Key Terms
دور
موقوف
انقضاء
ما لا نهاية له

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The past could be infinite because every day is preceded by another; infinity is just an endless series.
Correction: Razi shows that an infinite past would make the present day impossible, because it would require completing an infinite series to reach today. Since today exists, the series cannot be infinite.

Checkpoint
What is the condition for today's occurrence according to the sixth proof?
A) The completion of infinite past cycles.
B) The beginning of movement.
C) The existence of eternity.
D) The death of the universe.

Key Takeaway
Today's existence proves that the past cannot be infinite; thus the universe must have had a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If today's existence depends on a finite past, then your very existence in this moment is a sign that the universe had a beginning. Does that beginning point you to the One who brought it into existence? How does this truth affect your daily reliance on Him?
?
The proof shows that an infinite past is impossible, meaning that you cannot trace your lineage or history back forever. There must be a first cause. Have you ever truly paused to consider that your own life is not self-originating? What would it mean for your sense of independence if you truly believed that?
?
Razi's proof is not just intellectual; it demands a response. If the universe had a beginning and you are part of it, then your existence is contingent. Are you living as if you are contingent on Allah every moment, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What one change in your daily routine would reflect your belief that you exist only because of the First Cause?

The Need to Prove Rest as an Existent Attribute
Razi explains why proving rest is an existent attribute is a prerequisite for arguing against eternal rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi must first prove that rest is an existent attribute before arguing that it cannot be eternal, and how the objection about the world's temporal origination illustrates the problem.

Core Idea
Unless rest is a real attribute, the argument against eternal rest collapses.

This lesson unpacks the initial problem Razi faces. He wants to prove that bodies cannot have been at rest eternally, but he first needs to establish that rest is an actual attribute—not just the absence of motion. The philosophers treat it as a mere privation, which would render his key premise invalid. Razi shows that if rest were non-existence, then the principle 'what is eternal cannot cease' would not apply, because non-existence can cease in eternity. He illustrates this with a counterexample about the world's creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Razi states the problem and the structure of his reasoning.
2
Understand the philosophers' position
The philosophers claim rest is just absence of motion. Why does this matter for Razi's argument against eternal rest?
Razi's argument assumes that what is eternal cannot cease; if rest is non-existence, that principle doesn't apply.
3
Grasp the proof structure
Razi wants to argue: if rest were eternal, its removal would be impossible. But we observe removal, so rest is not eternal. However, if rest is non-existence, this fails because non-existence can cease in eternity.
4
Analyze the counterexample
Razi presents an objection about the world's temporal origination to show that the principle 'what is eternal cannot cease' must be restricted to existent things. Work through the logic step by step.
The objection: if the world is created, its non-existence was eternal, yet that non-existence ceased when the world came into being.
5
Connect to the need
Thus, to use the principle against eternal rest, we must first prove rest is an existent attribute. This sets up the proof in the next lesson.
The need is established; the proof follows in the next part of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة في بيان أنه يمتنع كون الأجسام ساكنة في الأزل
واعلم أنا نحتاج في هذا المقام إلى إقامة الدلالة على أن السكون أمر وجودي. والفلاسفة يزعمون : أنه عبارة عن عدم الحركة عما من شأنه أن يتحرك. وإنما افتقرنا إلى إثبات هذه المقدمة لأن دليلنا على أن السكون يمتنع أن يكون أزليا : هو أن نقول : لو كان السكون أزليًا لما جاز زواله، وقد جاز زواله فوجب أن لا يكون أزليا . ولو كان السكون أمرًا عدميًا، لما هذا الكلام، لأن زوال العدم في الأزل جائز بالاتفاق. إذ لو لم يجز ذلك، لبطل القول بحدوث العالم. فإن للسائل أن يقول : لو كان العالم محدثًا لكان عدمه أزليّا . ولو كان عدمه أزليّا لما زال بسبب طريان الوجود. وحيث وجد العالم علمنا أن عدم العالم ما كان أزليّا. فإن لم يكن عدمه أزليا كان وجوده أزليا، فكان العالم قديما. فثبت أنه لا يمكننا أن نقول : كل ما كان أزليا امتنع زواله بل يجب تخصيص هذه الدعوى بالأمور الوجودية. فيقال: كل ما كان موجودا في الأزل امتنع زواله. فإذا كان كذلك، فحينئذ نفتقر إلى إثبات كون السكون أمرًا وجوديًا، حتى يستقيم الكلام.
إذا عرفت ذلك، فنقول : الدليل على السكون أمر وجودي أنا نرى الجسم الواحد يصير ساكنا بعد أن كان متحركًا. وبالعكس. فتبدل هاتين الحالتين مع بقاء الذات في الحالتين يقتضي كون إحدى هاتين الحالتين أمرًا وجوديًا ؛ وإذا ثبت ذلك لزم كون كل واحد منهما أمرًا وجوديًا . وذلك لأن الحركة عبارة عن الحصول في الحيز ، بعد أن كانت في حيز آخر، والسكون عبارة عن الحصول في الحيز، بعد أن كان في نفس ذلك الحيز . فالحركة والسكون متساويان في تمام الماهية وإنما الاختلاف بينهما في كون الحركة مسبوقة بحالة أخرى، وكون السكون ليس كذلك. وكون الشيء مسبوقاً بغيره، وصف عرضي. والأوصاف العرضية لا تقدح في اتحاد الماهية. فثبت أن الحركة والسكون متساويان في تمام الماهية والحقيقة فإذا كان أحدهما وصفًا ثبوتيًا لزم كون الآخر ثبوتيًا قطعًا . فثبت بما ذكرنا أن السكون وصف ثبوتي.
The Third Introduction: On the Explanation that it is Impossible for Bodies to be at Rest in Eternity
Know that we need in this context to establish the proof that rest (sukūn) is an existent matter (amr wujūdī). The philosophers claim that it is an expression for the absence of movement from that which is capable of moving. And we needed to establish this premise because our proof that rest cannot be eternal is that we say: If rest were eternal, its removal would not be possible. But its removal is possible, so it must not be eternal. But if rest were a non-existent matter (amr 'adamī), this statement would not apply, because the removal of non-existence in eternity is unanimously permissible. For if that were not permissible, the statement on the temporal origination of the world would be invalid. For the questioner may say: If the world were temporally originated, its non-existence would be eternal. And if its non-existence were eternal, it would not be removed by the occurrence of existence. And since the world exists, we know that the non-existence of the world was not eternal. If its non-existence was not eternal, then its existence is eternal, so the world would be eternal. Thus, it is established that we cannot say: Everything that is eternal, its removal is impossible. Rather, this claim must be specified to existent matters. So it is said: Everything that existed in eternity, its removal is impossible. If that is so, then we need to establish that rest is an existent matter, in order for the statement to be sound.
Once you understand that, we say: The proof that rest is an existent matter is that we see the same body becoming at rest after it had been moving, and vice versa. The alternation of these two states while the substance (dhāt) remains in both states requires that one of these two states be an existent matter. And if that is established, then it necessarily follows that each of them is an existent matter. That is because movement is an expression for obtaining in a location after having been in another location, and rest is an expression for obtaining in a location after having been in the same location. Thus, movement and rest are equal in the complete essence (tamām al-māhiyya), and the difference between them is only in the fact that movement is preceded by another state while rest is not. And the fact that a thing is preceded by another is an accidental attribute (waṣf 'araḍī). Accidental attributes do not affect the unity of the essence. So it is established that movement and rest are equal in the complete essence and reality. Therefore, if one of them is a positive attribute (waṣf thubūtī), it necessarily follows that the other is also positive. So it is established by what we have mentioned that rest is a positive attribute.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument establishing that rest (sukūn) is a positive attribute (amr wujūdī) and not a mere privation (amr 'adamī). First, the author explains why this is needed: if rest were merely the absence of movement, then the argument that rest cannot be eternal would fail because non-existence can cease in eternity without contradiction. He illustrates this with an objection about the world's temporal origination: if the world's non-existence were eternal, it could not cease, but it did cease, so the world would be eternal—showing that the claim 'what is eternal cannot cease' only applies to existent things. Therefore, to use this claim against eternal rest, rest must be an existent attribute. The proof itself: we observe a body alternating between rest and motion while the body (dhāt) remains the same. This alternation indicates that one of these states must be real (existent) because change between non-existence and existence of a non-existent is not true change in the subject. Since motion is clearly real, rest must also be real. Additionally, both motion and rest share the same essence (māhiyya): both are 'obtaining in a location' (ḥuṣūl fī al-ḥayyiz), differing only in whether that location is the same or different from the previous one. This difference is accidental (ʿaraḍī). Since accidental attributes do not affect the essence identity, if motion is a positive attribute, rest must also be positive. The conclusion: rest is a positive attribute (waṣf thubūtī).

Key Terms
سكون
وجودي
عدمي
أزلي
زوال
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that rest is definitely non-existence (absence of motion) and that this is obvious.
Correction: Razi shows that this assumption would undermine the argument against eternal rest; therefore it must be proven that rest is existent.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi need to prove that rest is an existent attribute before arguing that it cannot be eternal?
A) Because if rest were non-existence, its removal in eternity would be possible, invalidating the argument.
B) Because the philosophers claim it is non-existence and he must refute them.
C) Because the body remains the same during rest and motion.
D) Because rest is always present.

Key Takeaway
Razi must establish rest as an existent attribute to make his argument against eternal rest valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that assuming rest is merely the absence of motion would invalidate the argument against eternal rest. In your own life, have you ever assumed that a lack of something (like rest being nothing) is harmless, when in fact it undermines a key principle of your faith? How does this call you to examine the foundations of what you believe?
?
The proof that rest is an existent attribute is a prerequisite for a larger argument. Are there prerequisites in your own understanding of Allah that you have neglected, assuming they don't matter? Will you now revisit them, even if it requires difficult study?
?
Razi shows that non-existence can cease in eternity, using the creation of the world as an example. This means that nothing created is truly eternal except Allah. How does this truth make you feel your own contingency and dependence on Allah every moment? Does it lead you to thank Him for the existence He grants you now?

The Proof that Rest is a Positive Attribute
Razi provides a two-part proof that rest is an existent attribute based on observed change and essence identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's two-part proof that rest is a positive attribute: first from the observation of alternation between motion and rest, and second from the essence identity of motion and rest.

Core Idea
Rest and motion share the same essence; if motion is real, rest must be real.

Having established the need to prove rest is existent, Razi now presents the proof. He reasons from observable change: a single body alternates between motion and rest while remaining the same substance. This alternation implies that both states are real attributes. Additionally, motion and rest have the same definition (obtaining in a location), differing only in whether the location is new or the same. This difference is accidental, so they share the same essence. Since motion is clearly a positive attribute, rest must be too.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the two parts: observation of alternation and the essence argument.
2
Understand the observation argument
We see a body become still after moving and move after being still. The substance remains the same. The change between these states requires that both states be real; otherwise the change would not be a change in the subject.
If one state were non-existent, the transition from non-existence to existence would not constitute a change in the subject's attributes.
3
Grasp the essence argument
Define motion and rest: both are obtaining in a location. The only difference is that motion is preceded by a different location, rest by the same. This difference is accidental (ʿaraḍī).
Accidental attributes do not affect the identity of the essence (māhiyya).
4
Apply the logic
Since motion and rest have the same essence, and motion is known to be a positive attribute, rest must also be positive.
5
Conclude
Thus, rest is a positive attribute (waṣf thubūtī). This allows Razi to use his argument against eternal rest.
The argument against eternal rest can now proceed.

The Third Introduction: On the Explanation that it is Impossible for Bodies to be at Rest in Eternity
Know that we need in this context to establish the proof that rest (sukūn) is an existent matter (amr wujūdī). The philosophers claim that it is an expression for the absence of movement from that which is capable of moving. And we needed to establish this premise because our proof that rest cannot be eternal is that we say: If rest were eternal, its removal would not be possible. But its removal is possible, so it must not be eternal. But if rest were a non-existent matter (amr 'adamī), this statement would not apply, because the removal of non-existence in eternity is unanimously permissible. For if that were not permissible, the statement on the temporal origination of the world would be invalid. For the questioner may say: If the world were temporally originated, its non-existence would be eternal. And if its non-existence were eternal, it would not be removed by the occurrence of existence. And since the world exists, we know that the non-existence of the world was not eternal. If its non-existence was not eternal, then its existence is eternal, so the world would be eternal. Thus, it is established that we cannot say: Everything that is eternal, its removal is impossible. Rather, this claim must be specified to existent matters. So it is said: Everything that existed in eternity, its removal is impossible. If that is so, then we need to establish that rest is an existent matter, in order for the statement to be sound.
Once you understand that, we say: The proof that rest is an existent matter is that we see the same body becoming at rest after it had been moving, and vice versa. The alternation of these two states while the substance (dhāt) remains in both states requires that one of these two states be an existent matter. And if that is established, then it necessarily follows that each of them is an existent matter. That is because movement is an expression for obtaining in a location after having been in another location, and rest is an expression for obtaining in a location after having been in the same location. Thus, movement and rest are equal in the complete essence (tamām al-māhiyya), and the difference between them is only in the fact that movement is preceded by another state while rest is not. And the fact that a thing is preceded by another is an accidental attribute (waṣf 'araḍī). Accidental attributes do not affect the unity of the essence. So it is established that movement and rest are equal in the complete essence and reality. Therefore, if one of them is a positive attribute (waṣf thubūtī), it necessarily follows that the other is also positive. So it is established by what we have mentioned that rest is a positive attribute.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument establishing that rest (sukūn) is a positive attribute (amr wujūdī) and not a mere privation (amr 'adamī). First, the author explains why this is needed: if rest were merely the absence of movement, then the argument that rest cannot be eternal would fail because non-existence can cease in eternity without contradiction. He illustrates this with an objection about the world's temporal origination: if the world's non-existence were eternal, it could not cease, but it did cease, so the world would be eternal—showing that the claim 'what is eternal cannot cease' only applies to existent things. Therefore, to use this claim against eternal rest, rest must be an existent attribute. The proof itself: we observe a body alternating between rest and motion while the body (dhāt) remains the same. This alternation indicates that one of these states must be real (existent) because change between non-existence and existence of a non-existent is not true change in the subject. Since motion is clearly real, rest must also be real. Additionally, both motion and rest share the same essence (māhiyya): both are 'obtaining in a location' (ḥuṣūl fī al-ḥayyiz), differing only in whether that location is the same or different from the previous one. This difference is accidental (ʿaraḍī). Since accidental attributes do not affect the essence identity, if motion is a positive attribute, rest must also be positive. The conclusion: rest is a positive attribute (waṣf thubūtī).

Key Terms
سكون
وجودي
حركة
ذات
ماهية
وصف ثبوتي
عرضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that rest is merely the absence of motion and therefore not a real attribute.
Correction: Razi proves that rest is a positive attribute by showing it shares the same essence as motion, which is undeniably real.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's proof, why does the fact that motion and rest have the same essence imply that rest is a positive attribute?
A) Because motion is obviously a positive attribute, and essence identity forces rest to be positive too.
B) Because rest is preceded by motion.
C) Because the body remains the same during both states.
D) Because the philosophers claim rest is non-existent.

Key Takeaway
Rest is a positive attribute because it shares the same essence as motion, which is undeniably real.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a moment of stillness in your day, do you consider it a mere void or a positive state of being present? How does recognizing rest as a positive attribute change your awareness of Allah's constant preservation of your existence in every state?
?
Your body remains the same despite alternating between motion and rest. Reflect on your own core identity—your soul—amidst the constant changes of life. Are you anchored in your substance as a servant of Allah, or do you let accidental attributes define you? What would it take to realign?
?
If rest is a positive attribute, then your times of inactivity are not empty—they are filled with existence. How will you use your next moment of stillness to connect with the Real, rather than waste it in distraction? Commit to one concrete action.

The Conditional: Why Eternal Rest Cannot Cease
Razi proves that any eternal existent is unceasing, using a causal analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's causal argument that anything existing from pre-eternity cannot cease to exist, and how this applies to rest.

Core Idea
Eternal existence entails necessary persistence.

In this passage, Razi builds on the earlier premise that rest is a positive attribute to prove that it cannot be eternal. He sets up a conditional: if rest were eternal, its cessation would be impossible. Since we observe that rest can cease, it cannot be eternal. To justify the impossibility of cessation, he examines the causal structure of eternal beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage from the start up to 'فثبت بما ذكرنا: أن كل ما كان موجودا في الأزل فإنه يمتنع زواله'. This covers the entire conditional argument.
Notice the logical structure: conditional, then division into two cases.
2
Trace the conditional logic
Identify the if-then structure and how Razi derives that eternal rest would be unceasing. Then note the observed premise that rest can cease, leading to the conclusion that rest is not eternal.
The conditional hinges on the impossibility of cessation for any eternal existent.
3
Analyze the causal argument
Examine the sub-arguments: the two categories (necessary vs. possible), the rejection of a free agent as cause, and the conclusion that a necessitating cause leads to unceasing existence.
Focus on why a free agent cannot cause an eternal thing and why a necessitating cause makes the effect necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قامت الحجة على صحة هذه المقدمة، فلنرجع إلى المطلوب. فنقول: لو كان السكون أزليا لامتنع زواله ولا يمتنع زواله فوجب أن لا يكون أزليا. بيان الشرطية: أنه بتقدير أن يكون أزليا، فإما أن يكون واجبا لذاته، أو ممكنا لذاته. فإن كان واجبا لذاته ظهر أنه يجب أن لا يزول أصلا. وإن كان ممكنا لذاته فالمؤثر فيه إما أن يكون فاعلا بالاختيار أو موجبا بالذات والأول محال؛ لأن الفاعل بالاختيار إنما يفعل بواسطة القصد والاختيار والقصد إلى تكوين الشيء، لا يتحقق إلا عند عدمه، أو حال حدوثه، وعلى التقديرين فكل ما يقع بالفاعل المختار كان محدثا. والقديم لا يكون محدثا، فيمتنع أن يكون المؤثر في وجود القديم فاعلا مختارا. فثبت أنه لو كان له مؤثر لكان ذلك المؤثر لا بد وأن يكون موجبا بالذات. ثم ذلك الموجب إن كان ممكنا عاد الكلام فيه، وإن كان واجبا فإن لم يتوقف تأثيره في وجود ذلك القديم على شرط أصلا لزم من وجوب تلك العلة وجوب وجود المعلول. فيلزم امتناع العدم على ذلك القديم وإن توقف تأثيره على شرط فذلك الشرط لا بد وأن يكون المؤثر فيه موجبا بالذات وواجبا بالذات وإلا عاد المحال المذكور. وحينئذ يلزم امتناع التغير على العلة، وعلى شرط العلة امتناع التغير على المعلول. فثبت بما ذكرنا: أن كل ما كان موجودا في الأزل فإنه يمتنع زواله. وإنما قلنا: إن الزوال على السكون جائز. وذلك لأن كل متحيز فإنه يمكن خروجه عن حيزه، وبتقدير خروجه عن حيزه فإنه يبطل ذلك السكون. بيان المقام الأول من وجهين: الأول: أن كل متحيز يفرض فإنه إما يكون مركبا أو بسيطا. فإن كان مركبا فإنه لا بد وأن يحصل فيه بسائط. فإذا أخذنا بسيطا واحدا من تلك البسائط فلا شك أنه يفرض فيه جانبان وطبيعة كل واحد من ذينك الجانبين مساوية لطبيعة الجانب الآخر، وإلا لكان ذلك الجزء مركبا. وقد فرضناه بسيطا هذا خلف. وإن كانت طبيعة كل واحد من ذينك الجانبين مساوية لطبيعة الجانب الآخر، وكل شيئين متساويين، وجب أن يصح على كل واحد منهما ما يصح على الآخر، فإذن الجانب الذي يلاقيه أو يحاذيه بيمينه يصح أن يلاقيه أو يحاذيه بيساره، ولا يمكن ذلك إلا إذا انقلب في حيزه، حتى يصير يمينه يسارا، أو يساره يمينا. فثبت أن كل جزء يفرض عليه الحركة. وإذا تحرك، فقد بطل ذلك السكون؛ لأنه لا معنى للسكون، إلا ذلك الحصول، وإذا بطل ذلك الحصول فقد بطل ذلك السكون.
And when the proof for the correctness of this premise is established, let us return to the desired conclusion. We say: if rest were eternal, its cessation would be impossible; but its cessation is not impossible, so it must not be eternal. Explanation of the conditional: assuming it is eternal, then either it is necessary by itself (wājib bi-dhātih) or possible by itself (mumkin bi-dhātih). If it is necessary by itself, it is apparent that it must not cease at all. If it is possible by itself, then the efficient cause (muʾaththir) of it is either a freely acting agent (fāʿil bi-l-ikhtiyār) or a necessitating agent by essence (mūjib bi-dh-dhāt). The first is impossible because the freely acting agent only acts through intent and choice, and intent to bring about a thing is only realized at its non-existence or at the moment of its origination; in both cases, whatever occurs by a freely acting agent is temporally originated (muḥdath). The eternal is not temporally originated, so it is impossible that the cause of existence of the eternal be a freely acting agent. Thus it is established that if it has a cause, that cause must be a necessitating agent by essence. Then that necessitating agent, if it is possible, the argument recurs; if it is necessary, then if its effect in bringing about that eternal does not depend on any condition at all, then from the necessity of that cause follows the necessity of the existence of the effect, so impossibility of non-existence necessarily attaches to that eternal. If its effect depends on a condition, then that condition must also have a cause that is necessitating by essence and necessary by essence, otherwise the aforementioned impossibility recurs. Then impossibility of change attaches to the cause, and impossibility of change attaches to the effect. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: that everything that existed in pre-eternity is impossible to cease. We only said that cessation of rest is possible. That is because every extended thing (mutaḥayyiz) can exit its location; and assuming it exits its location, that rest is nullified. Elucidation of the first point from two aspects: First: every extended thing that can be assumed is either composite or simple. If it is composite, it must contain simples. If we take one simple from those simples, there is no doubt that two sides (jānibān) can be assumed in it, and the nature of each of those two sides is equal to the nature of the other side; otherwise that part would be composite, and we have assumed it simple — a contradiction. If the nature of each of those two sides is equal to the nature of the other side, and any two equal things must have what is true of one also true of the other, then the side that faces or is adjacent to it on its right can face or be adjacent to it on its left; and that is only possible if it rotates in its location, so that its right becomes left or its left becomes right. Thus it is established that every part can be assumed to have motion. And if it moves, then that rest is nullified; because rest means nothing other than that 'being in location' (al-ḥuṣūl), and if that 'being' is nullified, then that rest is nullified.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is the core proof that eternal rest is impossible, following the established premise that rest is a positive attribute. Razi sets up a conditional: if rest were eternal, its cessation would be impossible; but observation shows rest can cease, so it cannot be eternal. He then justifies the impossibility of cessation for an eternal existent through a detailed analysis. He divides the possibilities: if the eternal rest is necessary by itself, it cannot cease. If it is possible by itself, it requires a cause. That cause cannot be a free agent because free agents only produce temporal things. So the cause must be a necessitating agent by essence. If that cause is itself necessary and unconditioned, its effect is necessary and eternal, thus unceasing. If it depends on a condition, the condition must similarly be necessitated, leading to an infinite chain that still prevents change. Thus any eternal existent is unceasing. Then Razi argues that rest can cease: any body (mutaḥayyiz) can move out of its location. He provides a detailed proof (first of two aspects) using the composition of bodies: every body is either composite or simple; if composed of simples, each simple has equal sides; equality implies that what is true of one side is true of another, so a simple can rotate such that its right side becomes left, which entails motion. If motion occurs, the previous rest (which is simply 'being in that location') is nullified. Hence rest can cease. Therefore, rest cannot be eternal. This concludes the third introduction's proof.

Key Terms
واجب (wājib)
ممكن (mumkin)
فاعل بالاختيار (fāʿil bi-l-ikhtiyār)
موجب بالذات (mūjib bi-dh-dhāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that a contingent eternal thing could cease on its own or by a free agent.
Correction: Razi argues that any eternal existent, whether necessary or contingent, has a cause that necessitates its existence, making cessation impossible. A free agent cannot cause an eternal thing because free agency implies temporality.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that a freely acting agent (fāʿil bi-l-ikhtiyār) cannot cause an eternal existent?
A) Because a free agent only acts when the thing is non-existent or at the moment of origination, which implies temporality.
B) Because Allah is eternal and cannot be an agent.
C) Because free agents are not real causes.
D) Because eternal things are self-caused.

Key Takeaway
Anything existing from pre-eternity is necessary in its existence and cannot cease; therefore, if rest were eternal it could not cease, but rest can cease, so rest is not eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Only Allah is eternal and unceasing. What is one temporal thing you have been treating as if it will never end—a relationship, a status, your health? How does this lesson unmask the illusion of permanence in your heart?
?
Razi proves that anything caused by a free agent must be temporally originated. If Allah created the universe as a free act of will, what does this imply about the universe's relationship to time? How should this affect your awe of the Creator's power and freedom?
?
Imagine you truly lived as though only the Eternal is worthy of your ultimate trust. What is one practical change you must make before the day ends to align your life with that truth?

The Possibility of Rest Ceasing
Razi proves that any body can change its location, thus rest can cease.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's philosophical proof that any body can move, thus rest can cease.

Core Idea
Bodies are capable of motion because of their structure.

After establishing that anything eternal cannot cease, Razi now shows that rest can cease because bodies can move. He provides a detailed argument based on the composition of bodies: whether composite or simple, each part has equal sides, allowing rotation and hence motion. This proves that rest is not permanent, and therefore not eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic from 'وإنما قلنا: إن الزوال على السكون جائز' to the end. This covers the proof that rest can cease.
Notice the two aspects mentioned; only the first is fully given, but the second is indicated.
2
Understand the claim and its basis
Razi claims that every extended thing (mutaḥayyiz) can exit its location, which would nullify rest. He supports this with an argument about simple parts.
Focus on the concept of 'sides' (jānibān) and equality of nature.
3
Follow the first aspect's reasoning
Trace the argument: every body is either composite or simple. If composite, it has simples. A simple has two sides with equal natures; if natures are equal, what is possible for one is possible for the other. So a side can become the other side via rotation, which is motion. Motion nullifies rest.
This is a classic kalam argument for the possibility of motion in bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قامت الحجة على صحة هذه المقدمة، فلنرجع إلى المطلوب. فنقول: لو كان السكون أزليا لامتنع زواله ولا يمتنع زواله فوجب أن لا يكون أزليا. بيان الشرطية: أنه بتقدير أن يكون أزليا، فإما أن يكون واجبا لذاته، أو ممكنا لذاته. فإن كان واجبا لذاته ظهر أنه يجب أن لا يزول أصلا. وإن كان ممكنا لذاته فالمؤثر فيه إما أن يكون فاعلا بالاختيار أو موجبا بالذات والأول محال؛ لأن الفاعل بالاختيار إنما يفعل بواسطة القصد والاختيار والقصد إلى تكوين الشيء، لا يتحقق إلا عند عدمه، أو حال حدوثه، وعلى التقديرين فكل ما يقع بالفاعل المختار كان محدثا. والقديم لا يكون محدثا، فيمتنع أن يكون المؤثر في وجود القديم فاعلا مختارا. فثبت أنه لو كان له مؤثر لكان ذلك المؤثر لا بد وأن يكون موجبا بالذات. ثم ذلك الموجب إن كان ممكنا عاد الكلام فيه، وإن كان واجبا فإن لم يتوقف تأثيره في وجود ذلك القديم على شرط أصلا لزم من وجوب تلك العلة وجوب وجود المعلول. فيلزم امتناع العدم على ذلك القديم وإن توقف تأثيره على شرط فذلك الشرط لا بد وأن يكون المؤثر فيه موجبا بالذات وواجبا بالذات وإلا عاد المحال المذكور. وحينئذ يلزم امتناع التغير على العلة، وعلى شرط العلة امتناع التغير على المعلول. فثبت بما ذكرنا: أن كل ما كان موجودا في الأزل فإنه يمتنع زواله. وإنما قلنا: إن الزوال على السكون جائز. وذلك لأن كل متحيز فإنه يمكن خروجه عن حيزه، وبتقدير خروجه عن حيزه فإنه يبطل ذلك السكون. بيان المقام الأول من وجهين: الأول: أن كل متحيز يفرض فإنه إما يكون مركبا أو بسيطا. فإن كان مركبا فإنه لا بد وأن يحصل فيه بسائط. فإذا أخذنا بسيطا واحدا من تلك البسائط فلا شك أنه يفرض فيه جانبان وطبيعة كل واحد من ذينك الجانبين مساوية لطبيعة الجانب الآخر، وإلا لكان ذلك الجزء مركبا. وقد فرضناه بسيطا هذا خلف. وإن كانت طبيعة كل واحد من ذينك الجانبين مساوية لطبيعة الجانب الآخر، وكل شيئين متساويين، وجب أن يصح على كل واحد منهما ما يصح على الآخر، فإذن الجانب الذي يلاقيه أو يحاذيه بيمينه يصح أن يلاقيه أو يحاذيه بيساره، ولا يمكن ذلك إلا إذا انقلب في حيزه، حتى يصير يمينه يسارا، أو يساره يمينا. فثبت أن كل جزء يفرض عليه الحركة. وإذا تحرك، فقد بطل ذلك السكون؛ لأنه لا معنى للسكون، إلا ذلك الحصول، وإذا بطل ذلك الحصول فقد بطل ذلك السكون.
And when the proof for the correctness of this premise is established, let us return to the desired conclusion. We say: if rest were eternal, its cessation would be impossible; but its cessation is not impossible, so it must not be eternal. Explanation of the conditional: assuming it is eternal, then either it is necessary by itself (wājib bi-dhātih) or possible by itself (mumkin bi-dhātih). If it is necessary by itself, it is apparent that it must not cease at all. If it is possible by itself, then the efficient cause (muʾaththir) of it is either a freely acting agent (fāʿil bi-l-ikhtiyār) or a necessitating agent by essence (mūjib bi-dh-dhāt). The first is impossible because the freely acting agent only acts through intent and choice, and intent to bring about a thing is only realized at its non-existence or at the moment of its origination; in both cases, whatever occurs by a freely acting agent is temporally originated (muḥdath). The eternal is not temporally originated, so it is impossible that the cause of existence of the eternal be a freely acting agent. Thus it is established that if it has a cause, that cause must be a necessitating agent by essence. Then that necessitating agent, if it is possible, the argument recurs; if it is necessary, then if its effect in bringing about that eternal does not depend on any condition at all, then from the necessity of that cause follows the necessity of the existence of the effect, so impossibility of non-existence necessarily attaches to that eternal. If its effect depends on a condition, then that condition must also have a cause that is necessitating by essence and necessary by essence, otherwise the aforementioned impossibility recurs. Then impossibility of change attaches to the cause, and impossibility of change attaches to the effect. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: that everything that existed in pre-eternity is impossible to cease. We only said that cessation of rest is possible. That is because every extended thing (mutaḥayyiz) can exit its location; and assuming it exits its location, that rest is nullified. Commentary
This passage is the core proof that eternal rest is impossible, following the established premise that rest is a positive attribute. Razi sets up a conditional: if rest were eternal, its cessation would be impossible; but observation shows rest can cease, so it cannot be eternal. He then justifies the impossibility of cessation for an eternal existent through a detailed analysis. He divides the possibilities: if the eternal rest is necessary by itself, it cannot cease. If that cause is itself necessary and unconditioned, its effect is necessary and eternal, thus unceasing. If it depends on a condition, the condition must similarly be necessitated, leading to an infinite chain that still prevents change. Thus any eternal existent is unceasing. Checkpoint
According to Razi, why can a simple body (basiṭ) move?
A) Because it is composed of smaller parts that can move independently.
B) Because it has two sides with equal natures, allowing rotation.

D) Because all bodies are composite.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If rest is not permanent and can cease at any moment, what does that say about the reliability of your daily routines and assumptions of stability? How should this affect your trust in Allah alone as the constant in your life?
?
Razi argues that even a simple part can move, implying nothing in the physical world is truly static. When you look at your own life, what areas have you considered 'at rest' or unchanging? How can you prepare yourself for the inevitable change that Allah has decreed?
?
The passage shows that rest is merely the fact of being in a location—not an intrinsic quality. Reflect on your actions: are you clinging to habits or states of being as if they were permanent? What would it mean for you to embrace that only Allah remains, and everything else is subject to change?

The Conditional Proof: Eternal Rest Cannot Cease
Razi sets up a conditional and analyzes the causes of eternal rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's conditional argument that if rest were eternal, it could not cease, and why a voluntary agent cannot cause an eternal effect.

Core Idea
Eternal things cannot change — their cause must be necessary.

Razi begins his proof for the temporality of bodies by focusing on the property of rest. He constructs a conditional: if rest were eternal, its cessation would be impossible. Before allowing any exceptions, he examines what it means for something to be eternal — either necessary or contingent — and shows that either way, an eternal entity cannot undergo cessation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Read the opening lines and locate the conditional 'if rest were eternal, its cessation would be impossible'.
Notice that Razi uses 'law' (if) to set up a hypothetical.
2
Trace the two possibilities for eternal rest
Razi splits the case: eternal rest is either necessary by itself (wajib li-dhatihi) or contingent by itself (mumkin li-dhatihi). Follow each branch to see why cessation becomes impossible.
For necessary: it simply cannot cease. For contingent: the cause must be a necessary agent.
3
Understand why a voluntary agent fails
Razi argues that a voluntary agent acts through intention and choice, which require the effect to be absent or coming into being — thus the effect is always originated (muhdath), not eternal.
This eliminates the possibility of a voluntary cause for an eternal rest.
4
See the consequence of a necessary agent
If the cause is necessary and unconditional, the effect is also necessary and unceasing. If conditional, the condition leads to an infinite chain of necessary causes, again preventing change.
Thus any eternal thing must be unchangeable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قامت الحجة على صحة هذه المقدمة، فلنرجع إلى المطلوب. فنقول : لو كان السكون أزليًا لامتنع زواله ولا يمتنع زواله فوجب أن لا يكون أزليا ، بيان الشرطية : أنه بتقدير أن يكون أزليّا ، فإما أن يكون واجبًا لذاته، أو ممكنا لذاته. فإن كان واجبًا لذاته ظهر أنه يجب أن لا يزول أصلاً. وإن كان ممكنا لذاته فالمؤثر فيه إما أن يكون فاعلا بالاختيار أو موجبًا بالذات والأول محال ؛ لأن الفاعل بالاختيار إنما يفعل بواسطة القصد والاختيار والقصد إلى تكوين الشيء، لا يتحقق إلا عند عدمه، أو حال حدوثه، وعلى التقديرين فكل ما يقع بالفاعل المختار كان محدثًا . والقديم لا يكون محدثًا، فيمتنع أن يكون المؤثر في وجود القديم فاعلا مختارا . فثبت أنه لو كان له مؤثر لكان ذلك المؤثر لا بد وأن يكون موجبًا بالذات . ثم ذلك الموجب إن كان ممكنا عاد الكلام فيه، وإن كان واجبًا فإن لم يتوقف تأثيره في وجود ذلك القديم على شرط أصلاً لزم من وجوب تلك العلة وجوب وجود المعلول. فيلزم امتناع العدم على ذلك القديم وإن توقف تأثيره على شرط فذلك الشرط لا بد وأن يكون المؤثر فيه موجبا بالذات وواجبا بالذات وإلا عاد المحال المذكور. وحينئذ يلزم امتناع التغير على العلة، وعلى شرط العلة امتناع التغير على المعلول. فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن كل ما كان موجودًا في الأزل فإنه يمتنع زواله. وإنما قلنا: إن الزوال على السكون جائز . وذلك لأن كل متحيز فإنه يمكن خروجه عن حيزه، وبتقدير خروجه عن حيزه فإنه يبطل ذلك السكون • بيان المقام الأول من وجهين : الأول : أن كل متحيز يفرض فإنه إما يكون مركبًا أو بسيطا . فإن كان مركبا فإنه لا بد وأن يحصل فيه بسائط. فإذا أخذنا بسيطا واحدًا من تلك البسائط فلا شك أنه يفرض فيه جانبان وطبيعة كل واحد من ذينك الجانبين مساوية لطبيعة الجانب الآخر، وإلا لكان ذلك الجزء مركبًا. وقد فرضناه بسيطا هذا خلف. وإن كانت طبيعة كل واحد من ذينك الجانبين مساوية لطبيعة الجانب الآخر، وكل شيئين متساويين، وجب أن يصح على كل واحد منهما ما يصح على الآخر، فإذن الجانب الذي يلاقيه أو يحاذيه بيمينه يصح أن يلاقيه أو يحاذيه بيساره، ولا يمكن ذلك إلا إذا انقلب في حيزه، حتى يصير يمينه يسارًا، أو يساره يمينا . فثبت أن كل جزء يفرض عليه الحركة. وإذا تحرك، فقد بطل ذلك السكون ؛ لأنه لا معنى للسكون، إلا ذلك الحصول، وإذا بطل ذلك الحصول فقد بطل ذلك السكون. الوجه الثاني : أنا نكتفي هنا بإلزام الخصم، وذلك لأن الأجسام عندهم إما فلكية أو عنصرية، أما الفلكيات فإنها عندهم متحركة على سبيل الوجوب . وأما العنصريات فكل واحد من أجزائها جائز الحركة. وعلى كلا التقديرين يثبت أن زوال السكون جائز. فثبت بما ذكرنا أن السكون لو كان أزليّا لما جاز زواله. وثبت أنه يجوز زواله، فيلزم أن لا يكون السكون أزليّا . ولما ثبت أنه يمتنع أن يكون الجسم في الأزل ساكنا، ويمتنع أن يكون في الأزل متحركًا، وثبت أنه لو كان أزليّا لكان إما ساكنا وإما متحركًا. ثبت أن الجسم يمتنع أن يكون أزليا ، وهو المطلوب. وهذا تمام هذا القول في تحرير هذا الدليل. وبالله التوفيق.
And when the proof is established for the correctness of this premise, let us return to the desired conclusion. We say: If rest were eternal, its cessation would be impossible, but its cessation is not impossible, so it must be that it is not eternal. Explanation of the conditional: On the assumption that it is eternal, then either it is necessary by itself or contingent by itself. If it is necessary by itself, it is evident that it must not cease at all. And if it is contingent by itself, then the effecter in it is either a voluntary agent or a necessary agent by essence. The first is impossible, because the voluntary agent only acts by means of intention and choice, and intention to bring something into existence is only realized at the moment of its non-existence or at the moment of its coming-to-be. On both estimations, whatever occurs by a voluntary agent is originated (muhdath). And the eternal (qadim) is not originated, so it is impossible that the effecter in the existence of the eternal be a voluntary agent. Thus it is established that if it had an effecter, that effecter must be a necessary agent by essence. Then, if that necessitator is contingent, the argument is repeated for it; and if it is necessary, then if its effect in the existence of that eternal is not dependent on any condition at all, the necessity of the cause necessitates the necessity of the existence of the effect, and thus the impossibility of non-existence for that eternal. And if its effect depends on a condition, then that condition must have an effecter that is necessary by essence and necessary by itself, otherwise the mentioned impossibility returns. In that case, the impossibility of change attaches to the cause and to the condition of the cause, and thus the impossibility of change attaches to the effect. So it is established by what we have mentioned that everything that exists in eternity (azal) is impossible to cease. And we said that cessation of rest is possible. That is because every extended thing (mutahayyiz) can exit its place, and on the assumption of its exit from its place, that rest is nullified. Explanation of the first station from two aspects: First: every supposed extended thing is either composite or simple. If it is composite, it must contain simples. If we take one simple from those simples, there is no doubt that two sides are assumed in it, and the nature of each of those two sides is equal to the nature of the other side; otherwise that part would be composite, but we assumed it simple—this is a contradiction. And if the nature of each of those two sides is equal to the nature of the other side, and any two equal things, it must be that what is valid for one of them is valid for the other. So then the side that meets or faces its right is valid to meet or face its left; this is not possible unless it rotates in its place, so that its right becomes left or its left becomes right. Thus it is established that every part is assumed to have motion. And if it moves, then that rest is nullified, because there is no meaning of rest except that state. And if that state is nullified, then that rest is nullified. The second aspect: We suffice here with pressing the opponent, because according to them, bodies are either celestial or elemental. As for celestial bodies, they are in motion out of necessity. As for elemental bodies, each of their parts is capable of motion. On both estimations, it is established that the cessation of rest is possible. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that if rest were eternal, its cessation would not be permissible. And it is established that its cessation is permissible, so it follows that rest is not eternal. And since it is established that it is impossible for a body to be at rest in eternity, and impossible for it to be in motion in eternity, and it is established that if it were eternal it would be either at rest or in motion, it is established that a body is impossible to be eternal—that is the desired conclusion. This completes this discourse on formulating this proof, and success is from Allah.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) for the temporal origination (huduth) of bodies, demonstrating that no body can be eternal (azali). The argument proceeds through several steps.

First, the author lays down a conditional proof: if rest (sukun) were eternal, its cessation would be impossible, but cessation is possible, so rest cannot be eternal. He explains the conditional: an eternal rest would be either necessary by itself (wajib li-dhatihi) or contingent by itself (mumkin li-dhatihi). If necessary, it cannot cease. If contingent, its cause must be either a voluntary agent (fa'il bi'l-ikhtiyar) or a necessitating agent (mujib bi'l-dhat). A voluntary agent cannot produce an eternal effect because its action involves intention and choice, which require non-existence or coming-to-be of the effect; thus its product is always originated (muhdath). Therefore, only a necessitating agent could be the cause of an eternal rest. But if the cause is necessary and unconditional, the effect is also necessary and unceasing; if conditional, the condition leads to an infinite regress of necessary causes, again preventing change. Hence eternal things are unchanging.

Second, he argues that rest can indeed cease because any extended thing (mutahayyiz) can leave its place, and leaving place nullifies rest. He supports this with two aspects (wajh). The first aspect analyzes the nature of bodies: every body is either simple or composite. Even a simple part (juz' basit) has two sides equal in nature, so what is valid for one side is valid for the other. Thus a simple part can rotate, meaning motion is possible, which would terminate its assumed rest. The second aspect appeals to the opponent's own physics: celestial bodies are necessarily in motion, and elemental bodies are capable of motion, so rest is not necessary.

Finally, the proof concludes that if body were eternal, it would have to be either at rest or in motion, but both are impossible in eternity (rest cannot be eternal as shown, and motion would require a prior state of rest or change, contradicting eternity). Therefore body cannot be eternal (azali). This is the desired conclusion (al-matlab). The passage ends with a statement that the proof is complete.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that eternal things can change if their cause changes.
Correction: Razi shows that if rest is eternal, its cause must be necessary and unchanging, so the effect itself cannot change.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that a voluntary agent cannot cause an eternal rest?
A) Because voluntary agents only act on composite bodies.
B) Because intention and choice require the effect to be non-existent or coming into being, so the effect is originated.
C) Because voluntary agents are themselves temporal.
D) Because rest is an accident and accidents cannot be caused voluntarily.

Key Takeaway
Razi's conditional proof shows that any eternal entity must be unchanging, so rest cannot be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson proves that only the Necessary Being is truly eternal and unchanging. What in your life do you treat as if it will never change—your health, relationships, or status? How does this proof expose the impermanence of those things and call you to anchor your hope solely in Allah?
?
If a voluntary agent cannot produce an eternal effect, what does that tell you about the nature of Allah's creation? How should this deepen your awe of His ability to originate the world from nothing, and how can you cultivate a sense of total dependence on Him today?
?
The argument shows that every contingent thing requires a chain of necessary causes ending in the Necessary Being. Does that logical dependence translate into a felt dependence in your daily life? What practical step can you take today to acknowledge that you are contingent upon Allah for every breath?

Allah's Existence
intermediate
8 min
Demonstrating the Possibility of Cessation of Rest and Conclusion
Razi provides two proofs that rest can cease and concludes body is temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's two arguments for why rest can cease, and how the contradiction with the earlier proof forces the conclusion that bodies are temporal.

Core Idea
Every body can move, so rest can cease — thus rest is not eternal.

Having established that eternal rest would be unceasing, Razi now demonstrates that rest in bodies can indeed cease. He provides two independent arguments: one from the nature of simple bodies and the possibility of rotation, and another from the opponent's own classification of bodies. The contradiction shows that rest cannot be eternal, and since a body cannot be without rest or motion in eternity, body itself cannot be eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and locate the two arguments
The passage presents 'two aspects' (wajh) for why rest can cease. Identify each one and understand its logic.
The first argument uses the nature of simples; the second appeals to the opponent's physics.
2
Understand the rotation argument
Razi argues that even a simple part of a body has hypothetical sides. Since the sides are equal, what applies to one side applies to the other, implying the part can rotate — hence motion is possible, terminating rest.
This is a subtle ontological argument about the nature of extension.
3
Understand the second argument from opponent's system
Razi notes that in the opponent's physics, celestial bodies are necessarily in motion, and elemental bodies are capable of motion. Therefore, rest is not permanent.
This is a dialectical argument, using the opponent's own beliefs.
4
See the final conclusion
Razi combines the impossibility of eternal rest with the possibility of cessation of rest, and adds that a body cannot be both at rest and in motion in eternity, so body cannot be eternal.
This completes the proof.

And when the proof is established for the correctness of this premise, let us return to the desired conclusion. We say: If rest were eternal, its cessation would be impossible, but its cessation is not impossible, so it must be that it is not eternal. Explanation of the conditional: On the assumption that it is eternal, then either it is necessary by itself or contingent by itself. If it is necessary by itself, it is evident that it must not cease at all. And if it is contingent by itself, then the effecter in it is either a voluntary agent or a necessary agent by essence. The first is impossible, because the voluntary agent only acts by means of intention and choice, and intention to bring something into existence is only realized at the moment of its non-existence or at the moment of its coming-to-be. On both estimations, whatever occurs by a voluntary agent is originated (muhdath). And the eternal (qadim) is not originated, so it is impossible that the effecter in the existence of the eternal be a voluntary agent. Thus it is established that if it had an effecter, that effecter must be a necessary agent by essence. Then, if that necessitator is contingent, the argument is repeated for it; and if it is necessary, then if its effect in the existence of that eternal is not dependent on any condition at all, the necessity of the cause necessitates the necessity of the existence of the effect, and thus the impossibility of non-existence for that eternal. And if its effect depends on a condition, then that condition must have an effecter that is necessary by essence and necessary by itself, otherwise the mentioned impossibility returns. In that case, the impossibility of change attaches to the cause and to the condition of the cause, and thus the impossibility of change attaches to the effect. So it is established by what we have mentioned that everything that exists in eternity (azal) is impossible to cease. And we said that cessation of rest is possible. That is because every extended thing (mutahayyiz) can exit its place, and on the assumption of its exit from its place, that rest is nullified. Explanation of the first station from two aspects: First: every supposed extended thing is either composite or simple. If it is composite, it must contain simples. If we take one simple from those simples, there is no doubt that two sides are assumed in it, and the nature of each of those two sides is equal to the nature of the other side; otherwise that part would be composite, but we assumed it simple—this is a contradiction. And if the nature of each of those two sides is equal to the nature of the other side, and any two equal things, it must be that what is valid for one of them is valid for the other. So then the side that meets or faces its right is valid to meet or face its left; this is not possible unless it rotates in its place, so that its right becomes left or its left becomes right. And if that state is nullified, then that rest is nullified. The second aspect: We suffice here with pressing the opponent, because according to them, bodies are either celestial or elemental. As for celestial bodies, they are in motion out of necessity. As for elemental bodies, each of their parts is capable of motion. On both estimations, it is established that the cessation of rest is possible. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that if rest were eternal, its cessation would not be permissible. Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) for the temporal origination (huduth) of bodies, demonstrating that no body can be eternal (azali). The argument proceeds through several steps.

First, the author lays down a conditional proof: if rest (sukun) were eternal, its cessation would be impossible, but cessation is possible, so rest cannot be eternal. He explains the conditional: an eternal rest would be either necessary by itself (wajib li-dhatihi) or contingent by itself (mumkin li-dhatihi). If necessary, it cannot cease. If contingent, its cause must be either a voluntary agent (fa'il bi'l-ikhtiyar) or a necessitating agent (mujib bi'l-dhat). A voluntary agent cannot produce an eternal effect because its action involves intention and choice, which require non-existence or coming-to-be of the effect; thus its product is always originated (muhdath). Therefore, only a necessitating agent could be the cause of an eternal rest. But if the cause is necessary and unconditional, the effect is also necessary and unceasing; if conditional, the condition leads to an infinite regress of necessary causes, again preventing change. Hence eternal things are unchanging.

Second, he argues that rest can indeed cease because any extended thing (mutahayyiz) can leave its place, and leaving place nullifies rest. He supports this with two aspects (wajh). The first aspect analyzes the nature of bodies: every body is either simple or composite. Even a simple part (juz' basit) has two sides equal in nature, so what is valid for one side is valid for the other. Thus a simple part can rotate, meaning motion is possible, which would terminate its assumed rest. The second aspect appeals to the opponent's own physics: celestial bodies are necessarily in motion, and elemental bodies are capable of motion, so rest is not necessary.

Finally, the proof concludes that if body were eternal, it would have to be either at rest or in motion, but both are impossible in eternity (rest cannot be eternal as shown, and motion would require a prior state of rest or change, contradicting eternity). Therefore body cannot be eternal (azali). This is the desired conclusion (al-matlab). The passage ends with a statement that the proof is complete.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the rotation argument only works for composite bodies.
Correction: Razi deliberately uses a simple part (juz' basit) to show that even in a simple body, motion is possible because of equal sides.

Checkpoint
What does Razi's first aspect (wajh) about a simple part demonstrate?
A) That simple parts cannot move because they have no parts.
B) That a simple part can rotate due to the equality of its sides.
C) That simple parts are eternal.
D) That rest is the natural state of simples.

Key Takeaway
By showing rest can cease, Razi completes the proof that bodies are temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your body is temporal and will inevitably perish, what is the one attachment to this world that you have been treating as permanent, and what concrete step will you take today to release it?
?
The proof shows that rest is not eternal—everything is subject to change. What area of your spiritual life have you left stagnant, assuming it will remain as is, and what movement will you initiate this week?
?
If your ultimate purpose lies beyond this temporal body, how are you currently investing your time and energy in a way that reflects that truth? What will you stop doing or start doing from this moment?
