Motion in Eternity: The Main Argument
A dialectical proof refuting the impossibility of motion in eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dialectical proof that motion in eternity is not impossible, and how the opponent's six proofs are refuted by a single argument about intrinsic vs. extrinsic impossibility.

Core Idea
Motion in eternity is not impossible; the burden is on the opponent to show why it is impossible.

Imam Razi presents a powerful dialectical argument against those who claim that motion in an eternal body is impossible. He grants the opponent's premise that an eternal body must be either at rest or in motion, then challenges their assertion that motion is impossible. Instead of engaging each of their six proofs individually, he offers a single logical counter-argument that cuts to the heart of the issue: is the impossibility intrinsic or extrinsic? This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent claims motion in eternity is impossible. Note the starting concession: an eternal body is either moving or at rest.
2
Understand the dilemma
The counter-argument presents a dilemma: is the impossibility intrinsic (in itself) or not? Follow each branch.
Intrinsic impossibility would mean motion never exists.
3
Trace the extrinsic branch
If not intrinsic, the impossibility must be due to something else. Examine whether that impediment is necessary or contingent.
Necessary impediment cannot cease; contingent leads to regress.
4
Conclusion
If motion is not impossible at all, the opponent's proofs are false.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا : أن الجسم لو كان أزليّا، لكان إما أن يكون ساكنا وإما أن يكون متحركًا. فلم قلتم : إنه يمتنع كون الجسم في الأزل متحركا ؟ والدلائل السنة التي عوّلْتُم عليها في بيان امتناع كون الجسم متحركًا في الأزل هي بأسرها معارضة بوجه واحد. أن وجود الحركة وتأثير المؤثر في إيجاد الحركة في الأزل وإما أن يكون ممتنعا وهو لعينه ولذاته وإما أن لا يكون ممتنعا لعينه ولذاته. فإن كان ممتنعا لذاته وجب أن لا يزول ذلك الامتناع قط؛ لأن ما الذات لا يزول فوجب أن لا توجد الحركة أصلاً . وأما إن كان وجود الحركة الأزلية وإيجاد الحركة في الأزل ليس ممتنعا لذاته فإما أن يكون ممتنعا لغيره، أو لا يكون كذلك. فإن كان ممتنعًا لغيره، فلذلك المانع إن كان واجبًا لذاته امتنع زواله. وكان يجب امتناع زوال ذلك الامتناع، وإن كان واجبًا أيضًا لغيره كان الكلام فيه كما في الأول. فيلزم التسلسل . وأما إن كان وجود الحركة الأزلية غير ممتنعة لذاته ولا لغيرها امتنع الحكم عليها بالامتناع ودلائلكم عليها تقضي بالامتناع . فكانت باطلة. لا يقال : المانع من حصول الحركة هو تحقيق مسمى الأزل؛ لأن الحركة تقتضي المسبوقية بالغير، والأزل ينافي المسبوقية بالغير فالجمع بينهما محال. لأنا نقول : مسمى الأزل حيث ثبت وتحقق إن كان واجب الثبوت لذاته امتنع زواله قط، فكان يجب أن لا يزول ذلك الامتناع أبدا، وإن لم يكن واجب الثبوت لذاته، كان ثبوته لأجل شيء آخر. والكلام في ذلك الثاني، كما في الأول. ولا يتسلسل، بل ينتهي بالآخرة إلى واجب الوجود لذاته. وحينئذ يلزم من امتناع زوال الواجب لذاته امتناع زوال مسمى الأزل، ويلزم من امتناع زواله أن لا يزول قط امتناع حدوث الحركة. ولما كان ذلك باطلاً، علمنا أن الحركة لا يمتنع حصولها في الأزل.
We grant that if the body were eternal, it would be either at rest or in motion. So why do you say that it is impossible for the body to be moving in eternity? The six proofs that you relied upon to show the impossibility of the body being moving in eternity are all countered by a single argument: The existence of motion and the effect of the agent in producing motion in eternity—either it is impossible in itself and for itself, or it is not impossible in itself and for itself. If it is impossible in itself, then that impossibility must never cease, because what is self-essential does not cease, so motion should never exist at all. But if the existence of eternal motion and the production of motion in eternity is not impossible in itself, then either it is impossible due to something else, or it is not. If it is impossible due to something else, then that impediment, if it is necessary in itself, its cessation is impossible, and the cessation of that impossibility would be impossible; if it is also necessary due to something else, the same discussion applies, leading to an infinite regress. But if the existence of eternal motion is not impossible in itself nor due to something else, then it cannot be judged as impossible, and your proofs that entail impossibility are false. One might object: 'The impediment to motion is the realization of the concept of eternity (azal), because motion requires being preceded by something else, and eternity negates being preceded by something else, so combining them is impossible.' We say: The concept of eternity, once established and realized, if its existence is necessary in itself, its cessation is impossible, so that impossibility would never cease; if its existence is not necessary in itself, its existence is due to something else, and the same discussion applies, but it does not lead to infinite regress; rather it ultimately ends with the Necessary Being in itself. And then, from the impossibility of the cessation of that which is necessary in itself follows the impossibility of the cessation of the concept of eternity, and from the impossibility of its cessation follows that the impossibility of motion's occurrence never ceases. Since that is false, we know that motion is not impossible in eternity.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical proof that motion in eternity is not impossible. The author grants that an eternal body would be either moving or at rest, and questions the opponent's claim that motion in eternity is impossible. He reduces the opponent's six proofs to one counter-argument: consider the existence of motion in eternity and its production by an agent. Either this is intrinsically impossible or not. If intrinsically impossible, it would never be possible, so motion would never exist—but it does exist in the world. If not intrinsically impossible, then the impossibility must be due to an extrinsic impediment. That impediment, if self-necessary, cannot cease, meaning motion could never occur; if it is contingent, an infinite regress looms. The only way out is for the impediment to ultimately depend on the Necessary Being, which would make the impossibility eternal—but this is false because motion does occur in time. Therefore, motion in eternity is not impossible. The passage also refutes an objection that the very concept of eternity prevents motion because motion requires temporal priority; the author replies that eternity itself must be either necessary or contingent, leading to a regress that ends with the Necessary Being, but the conclusion that the impossibility is eternal is false, so the objection fails.

Key Terms
أزل (azal)
ممتنع (mumtani')
واجب لذاته (wajib li-dhatih)
تسلسل (tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of motion in eternity is self-evident.
Correction: The passage shows that the impossibility is not intrinsic; it must be proven. If it were self-evident, the opponent's six proofs would be redundant.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the existence of motion in eternity is not impossible in itself, what is the consequence for the opponent's proofs?
A) They stand as valid.
B) They are shown to be false because they incorrectly attribute impossibility.
C) They are partially correct but need modification.
D) They are irrelevant because motion is not real.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's claim that motion in eternity is impossible collapses when subjected to the dilemma of intrinsic vs. extrinsic impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you ever assume that something is impossible just because it does not fit your mental model, without actually proving its impossibility? Where might you be doing this right now in your relationship with Allah?
?
This passage forces you to check the logical coherence of your beliefs. Are there any beliefs you hold about Allah's power or the world that you have never seriously examined? What would it take for you to genuinely test them?
?
If the opponent's proof fails, it means the door is open for a creator who can initiate motion in eternity. How does this change the way you think about your own origin? Does it stir a sense of accountability in you?

Objection and Refutation: Eternity and Motion
Refutation of an objection based on the concept of eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the objection that the concept of eternity itself prevents motion, and how this refutation ultimately points to the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
The concept of eternity does not inherently negate motion; it depends on whether eternity's existence is necessary or contingent.

After presenting the main argument, Imam Razi anticipates an objection: the very definition of eternity implies timelessness, and motion requires temporal priority, so they are incompatible. He responds by analyzing the nature of eternity itself. If it is necessary, its cessation is impossible, making motion forever impossible—but motion exists, so that cannot be. If eternity is contingent, it traces back to the Necessary Being, and the impossibility would still be eternal, which is false. Thus, the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector claims that the concept of eternity itself prevents motion because motion requires temporal priority.
2
Analyze the response
Imam Razi responds by examining whether the concept of eternity has necessary or contingent existence.
3
Follow the necessary branch
If eternity is necessary, its cessation is impossible, so the impossibility of motion would be eternal—but motion exists, so this is false.
4
Follow the contingent branch
If eternity is contingent, it leads to the Necessary Being, making the impossibility eternal as well, which is false.
5
Conclude the refutation
Since both branches lead to falsehood, the objection fails, and motion in eternity is not impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Razi's Eternal Non-Existent Condition Argument
Razi responds to the objection that the eternal cannot cease, using an eternal non-existent condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that an eternal thing can cease to exist if its existence depends on an eternal non-existent condition that ceases.

Core Idea
An eternal caused thing can cease when its eternal condition ceases.

Razi responds to the objection that an eternal being cannot cease to exist. He builds on the opponent's concession that eternal non-existence can cease, then introduces the concept of an eternal non-existent condition. Understanding this argument clarifies how divine causation can account for the beginning and end of created things without implying change in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once, noticing the key terms: العدم الأزلي, الشرط العدمي الأزلي, الواجب لذاته.
The passage is dense but logical.
2
Identify Razi's starting point
Notice that Razi begins by referencing the opponent's concession that eternal non-existence can cease. This is the foundation.
3
Understand the condition
Razi proposes that a cause's effect may depend on an eternal non-existent condition. This condition itself is non-existent but eternal.
Think of it as a precondition that must be fulfilled.
4
Trace the argument
Razi applies this: the Necessary Being is the cause of an eternal thing, but the effect is conditional on an eternal condition. When that condition ceases, the effect ceases.
Thus the eternal thing is not self-subsistent.
5
Reflect on the strength
Razi calls this a strong objection. Consider why it is effective against the opponent's claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : أنتم سلمتم أن العدم الأزلي جائز الزوال، ولا يمتنع أيضًا أن يكون تأثير العلة في محلولها موقوفًا على شرط عدمي أزلي. إذا ثبت هذا، فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون الواجب لذاته : علة لوجود ذلك الأزلي، إلا أن تأثيره في إيجابه كان موقوفًا على شرط عدمي أزلي، ثم إن ذلك الشرط العدمي الأزلي زال ولما زال شرط التأثير لا جرم زال الأثر. وهذا سؤال قوي.
We say: You have conceded that eternal non-existence is possible of ceasing, and it is also not impossible that the effect of the cause upon its effect depends on an eternal non-existent condition. If this is established, then we say: Why is it not permissible that the Necessary Being in Himself (al-wajib li-dhatihi) be the cause of the existence of that eternal [thing], except that His effect in bringing it into existence was conditional upon an eternal non-existent condition, then that eternal non-existent condition ceased, and since the condition of the effect has ceased, inevitably the effect has ceased. And this is a strong objection.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents a response to the opponent's claim that the eternal cannot cease. Razi first notes that the opponent has already conceded that eternal non-existence can cease. He then argues that it is not impossible for the effect of a cause to depend on a condition that is eternal but non-existent. Using this, he proposes a scenario: the Necessary Being (God) is the cause of an eternal thing, but His bringing it into existence was dependent on an eternal non-existent condition. When that condition ceases, the effect also ceases. Thus, an eternal thing can cease without implying any change in God. Razi concludes that this is a strong objection.

Key Terms
العدم الأزلي
الشرط العدمي الأزلي
الواجب لذاته
تأثير العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that a condition for an effect must be something positive that exists.
Correction: Razi introduces the idea of an eternal non-existent condition, showing that a condition can be the absence of something, not necessarily a positive entity.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's argument, why can an eternal thing cease to exist?
A) Because God's power is limited.
B) Because its existence depended on an eternal non-existent condition that ceased.
C) Because all eternal things are inherently perishable.
D) Because the cause changed its mind.

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument shows that an eternal caused thing can cease if its existence depends on a condition that is itself eternal but non-existent, and that condition ceases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your entire existence depends on conditions outside your control, including ones that have never been realized, are you truly living as though you rely on God every moment? Or have you become complacent, assuming your existence is self-sufficient? What would it mean for you to live with the constant awareness that you are contingent upon a condition that only God knows?
?
Razi speaks of an eternal non-existent condition—something that never was, yet its ceasing causes an effect to cease. Are there 'non-existent conditions' in your spiritual life: absent virtues, neglected duties, unfulfilled intentions—that are holding you back from drawing closer to God? Will you allow these absences to persist, or will you replace them with actions?
?
The argument shows that God's act can be conditional, yet He remains unchanging. How does this deepen your trust in God's wisdom? Are you willing to accept that God's delays or 'conditional' responses in your life are not due to His changing but due to conditions you may not see? Will you commit to seeking His conditions rather than demanding immediate fulfillment of your desires?

Razi's Objection: The Possibility of Necessary Rest
Razi argues that the opponent's claim that every body is originated is not proven.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's dialectical objection to the claim that every body is originated, based on the possibility that some bodies are necessarily at rest.

Core Idea
Some bodies may be eternally at rest—can't assume all rest is possible.

In this passage, Razi engages in a dialectical exchange with an opponent who argues that all bodies are originated because they undergo change. Razi concedes a premise but then introduces a counterexample that challenges the opponent's inference. This lesson examines his reasoning and its implications for proving the origin of bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how Razi begins with a concession ('We concede...') and then raises an objection.
The passage is short but dense; pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Identify the key moves
Identify the conceded premise (the eternal does not cease), the opponent's claim (every rest is possible), and Razi's counterexample (some bodies might be necessarily at rest).
3
Understand the objection's force
Reflect on why this objection blocks the opponent's conclusion. If some bodies could be necessarily at rest, then not all bodies are originated (some could be eternal).
This shows that the opponent's proof is incomplete unless they can rule out necessary rest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا : أن الثابت الأزلي لا يزول. فلم قلتم بأن كل سكون فإنه ممكن وهذا باطل. لأنا نشاهد بعض الأجسام متحركة. ولكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون بعض الأجسام مختصا بأحياز معينة، على سبيل الوجوب، بحيث يمتنع خروجها عن تلك الأحياز؟ فإنكم ما لم تبطلوا هذا الاحتمال لا يتم لكم إثبات أن كل جسم محدث.
We concede: that the eternal established does not cease. So why do you say that every rest is possible? This is false. For we observe some bodies moving. But why is it not permissible that some bodies are specified to particular places, in a necessary manner, such that their departure from those places is impossible? For unless you refute this possibility, you cannot prove that every body is originated.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage begins by conceding the premise that the eternal does not cease. Then Razi challenges the opponent's claim that every state of rest is possible. He points out that we observe some bodies moving, but that does not prove that all bodies can move. It is possible that some bodies are permanently and necessarily attached to specific places, unable to leave them. Unless the opponent disproves this possibility, he cannot conclude that all bodies are originated (because they might be eternal and resting).

Key Terms
السكون
الحركة
الوجوب
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that rest is always a possible state for any body.
Correction: Razi argues that rest could be necessary for some bodies, meaning not all rest is possible, which would undermine arguments for the origin of bodies.

Checkpoint
What is Razi's objection regarding the possibility of rest?
A) All bodies must be moving.
B) Some bodies may be necessarily at rest, so not all bodies are necessarily possible of motion.
C) Rest is impossible.
D) Every body is originated because it moves.

Key Takeaway
Razi challenges the assumption that all rest is possible, arguing that some bodies might be necessarily at rest, preventing proof that all bodies are originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi challenges the assumption that every rest is possible. In your life, what assumptions do you hold about yourself that might not be true—areas where you think change is always possible, but perhaps it is not? How will you honestly examine whether your spiritual 'rests' (like habits or beliefs) are truly possible to change or have become necessary for you?
?
The opponent's argument fails because he did not refute a possibility. What possibilities concerning your own faith—such as the existence of God or the truth of Islam—have you never seriously considered? Are you avoiding genuine inquiry because it might unsettle your current certainty? What step will you take today to investigate one such possibility?
?
Razi uses logic to deepen the argument, not to destroy faith. When you encounter objections to your beliefs, do you feel threatened or intellectually curious? How can you adopt Razi's dialectical approach to strengthen your understanding rather than defensiveness?

Razi's Response to the Objection on the Body's First Moment
Razi clarifies that the discussion concerns the body after it exists, not at first origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi responds to the objection about a body's state at the first moment by refocusing the discussion on the persistent body.

Core Idea
The argument is about the persistent body, not the first moment.

This short passage addresses a clever objection: if a body is neither moving nor at rest at the first moment of existence, then arguments about motion might seem irrelevant. Razi responds by clarifying that his discussion concerns the body after it has come into existence and endures—what he calls 'the persistent body'. This refocusing is crucial for understanding the theological argument about motion and pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the opponent's claim followed by Razi's reply.
Pay attention to the words 'first occurrence' vs 'persistent'.
2
Identify the shift in focus
Razi does not deny the opponent's claim but shifts the discussion from the first moment to the state of persistence. Understand why this shift is valid.
3
Connect to the broader argument
Razi mentions six proofs that a body cannot be moving in pre-eternity. Consider how the shift to the persistent body supports these proofs.
This shows the objection does not undermine the overall argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: الجسم في أول حدوثه ليس بمتحرك ولا ساكن قلنا : كلامنا في الجسم الباقي، ولا شك أن الجسم حال بقائه لا بد وأن يكون إما متحركًا وإما ساكنا . ثم إنا ذكرنا الدلائل الستة على أن الجسم يمتنع أن يكون متحركا في الأزل.
He said: The body at its first occurrence is neither moving nor at rest. We said: Our speech is about the body after it persists (al-baqi). There is no doubt that the body in the state of its persistence must be either moving or at rest. Moreover, we have mentioned the six proofs that the body is impossible to be moving in pre-eternity.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage responds to an objection: the opponent says that at the very first moment of existence, a body is neither moving nor at rest. Razi clarifies that the discussion is about the body after it persists (i.e., after it has come into existence and endures). In that state, it must be either moving or at rest. He also references earlier proofs that the body cannot be moving eternally.

Correction: Razi clarifies that the argument concerns the body after it persists, which must be either moving or at rest, so the objection misses the mark.

Distinguishing Possibility from Eternity
Razi refutes an argument for the eternity of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a thing conditioned by prior non-existence cannot be considered eternal even if its possibility has no beginning.

Core Idea
Possibility without beginning does not imply actual eternity.

Razi addresses a subtle objection: if the possibility of motion has no beginning, why isn't motion itself eternal? He distinguishes between the conditional validity of a thing and its actual existence. This passage clarifies a key logical distinction in arguments about creation and eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Let the author's own flow come through.
Notice the structure: objection presented, then answer.
2
Identify the objection
The opponent argues that because the possibility of motion has no beginning, motion itself must be eternal. Understand why this might seem plausible.
The opponent assumes that beginningless possibility guarantees beginningless existence.
3
Understand Razi's answer
Razi says the thing is conditioned by being preceded by non-existence. Its validity (as a possible thing) has no beginning, but the thing itself is not eternal because the condition of prior non-existence prevents it.
The key is the condition 'mسبوق بالعدم' (preceded by non-existence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : إنه لا بداية لصحة وجود الحركة فيلزم صحة كون الحركة أزلية قلنا : قد ذكرنا أن الشيء أخذ بشرط كونه مسبوقا بالعدم، فهو مع هذا الشرط لا أول لصحته، ثم إنه لم يلزم منه صحة كون هذا الشيء بهذا الشرط أزليا، فكذا ههنا.
He said: There is no beginning to the validity of motion's existence, so it follows that motion is eternal. We said: We have mentioned that a thing taken with the condition of being preceded by non-existence, with this condition it has no beginning to its validity, but it does not follow from that that this thing with this condition is eternal. Likewise here.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage answers an objection that if the possibility of motion has no beginning, then motion itself must be eternal. Razi clarifies that something can be conditioned by prior non-existence, meaning it is valid after non-existence. Its validity has no beginning (i.e., it is always valid after non-existence), but that does not imply that the thing itself is eternal. The condition prevents it from being eternal.

Key Terms
صحة الوجود
مسبوق بالعدم
أزلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something's possibility has no beginning, then that thing itself must be eternal.
Correction: Possibility is a property of the thing as a potential existence, not a statement about its actual existence. A thing conditioned by non-existence can have beginningless possibility but cannot be eternal.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi reject the inference that motion is eternal just because its possibility has no beginning?
A) Because possibility is not existence.
B) Because motion is impossible.
C) Because the possibility is conditioned by prior non-existence, which prevents actual eternity.
D) Because eternity is not a property of motion.

Key Takeaway
A thing's possibility may be beginningless, but if it is conditioned by prior non-existence, it cannot be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own death, you know it is possible at any moment. But does that knowledge actually change how you live today? Or do you treat it as a distant possibility rather than an inevitable actuality?

Reflect on a time you thought, 'I could do that good deed anytime,' yet you never did. What does Razi's distinction teach you about the gap between possibility and action?
?
Is there a sin you assume will be forgiven simply because forgiveness is possible? What does the condition of 'preceded by non-existence' teach you about the need for actual repentance?

The First Argument for the Finitude of Bodies
Razi presents a proof that bodies are finite, with two arguments; the first is complete, the second is introduced.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first argument for the finitude of bodies based on the impossibility of a first point of alignment with an infinite line.

Core Idea
Infinite bodies lead to logical contradictions like requiring a first point where none exists.

Razi presents a proof that bodies are finite, a necessary step to proving their temporal origination. He offers two geometric arguments; the first is given in full here. Using two parallel lines, one finite and one infinite, he shows that assuming an infinite body leads to an absurdity. Follow the logic carefully to see why the infinite must be rejected.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall flow of the argument.
Notice Razi's dense style; he presents a syllogism first, then the geometric proof.
2
Identify the key premises and conclusion
Identify the main claim: bodies are finite. Note the premise that finitude implies origination. Then focus on the first argument (al-wajh al-awwal).
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: assume infinite, derive contradiction, therefore finite.
3
Understand the geometric scenario
Visualize two parallel lines, one finite and one infinite. Consider rotating the finite line to align with the infinite line. Razi argues that this would require a first point of alignment on the infinite line, which is impossible.
Why is a first point impossible? Because an infinite line has no starting point; any assumed first point would be preceded by another.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
Notice how the contradiction is derived: the assumption leads to both that a first point must exist and that it cannot exist. This proves the assumption false.
This method is typical in kalam: demonstrating impossibility of the opposite to prove a necessary truth.
5
Note the incomplete second argument
The passage introduces a second argument but cuts off. It begins with an infinite line AB and two points in the middle, but the rest is missing. We will cover it when the text is complete.
For now, focus on mastering the first argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثاني: في إثبات حدود الأجسام : فنقول : الأجسام متناهية المقدار، وكل ما كان متناهيا في المقدار فهو محدث، فالأجسام محدثة. والذي يدل على أن الأجسام متناهية في المقدار وجهان: الوجه الأول: الأجسام لو كانت غير متناهية أمكن أن يفرض فيها خطان ،متوازيان أحدهما غير متناه والآخر متناه، فإذا زال الخط المتناهي عن الموازاة إلى المسامتة فلا بد وأن يحدث في الخط الذي هو غير متناه ،نقطة أول نقطة هي المسامتة، لكن ذلك محال في الخط الذي هو متناه ؛ لأن كل نقطة فرضناها فيه وجعلناها أول نقطة المسامتة كانت المسامتة مع النقطة التي فوقها، حاصلة قبل المسامتة معها، فإذن يلزم أن يحصل في الخط الغير المتناهي نقطة أول نقطة هي المسامتة، وأن لا يحصل وهو محال وهذا المحال إنما يلزم من فرضنا ذلك الخط غير متناه، وإذا فرض الخط غير متناه وجب أن يكون محالا باطلا. والوجه الثاني : في بيان أن الأجسام يجب أن تكون متناهية لو كانت الأجسام غير متناهية أمكننا أن نفرض فيها خطا غير متناه وليكن ذلك الخط خط (اب) ويمكننا أن نفرض في وسط هذا الخط نقطتين : إحداهما نقطة (ج) والأخرى نقطة (د)
The Second Proof: On Establishing the Limits of Bodies. We say: Bodies are finite in magnitude, and everything that is finite in magnitude is originated (muhdath), so bodies are originated. What indicates that bodies are finite in magnitude are two aspects: The first aspect: If bodies were infinite, it would be possible to assume in them two parallel lines, one infinite and the other finite. Then when the finite line moves from being parallel to being aligned (musāmata), there must occur in the infinite line a first point of alignment. But that is impossible in the finite line, because any point we assume in it and make it the first point of alignment, the alignment with the point above it would have occurred before alignment with it. So it follows that in the infinite line there occurs a first point of alignment and that it does not occur, which is impossible. This impossibility arises from our assumption that the line is infinite. So if the line is assumed to be infinite, it must be false and impossible. The second aspect: In explaining that bodies must be finite: if bodies were infinite, we could assume in them an infinite line, let that line be line AB, and we could assume in the middle of this line two points, one point J and another point D.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the finitude of bodies. It begins with the premise that bodies have finite magnitude, and finitude implies origination (temporal creation). Two arguments are given. The first argument uses two parallel lines, one finite and one infinite. When the finite line rotates to align with the infinite one, a first point of alignment should appear in the infinite line. But such a first point is impossible because in the infinite line there is no beginning. Therefore, the assumption of an infinite line is absurd, so bodies must be finite. The second argument is cut off; it appears to consider an infinite line AB with two points in its middle, likely to derive a contradiction. The proof is geometric and relies on the impossibility of an actual infinite.

Key Terms
متناهي
غير متناهي
محدث
حدود الأجسام
خطان متوازيان
مسامتة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An infinite physical body or line can exist without contradiction.
Correction: Razi shows that assuming an infinite line leads to the impossibility of defining a first point of alignment, proving that infinite bodies cannot exist.

Checkpoint
In the first argument for the finitude of bodies, what happens when a finite parallel line is moved to align with an infinite line?
A) The infinite line becomes finite
B) A first point of alignment would be required, which is impossible
C) The lines remain parallel
D) The finite line becomes infinite

Key Takeaway
Razi's first argument shows that the assumption of an infinite body leads to a logical contradiction, thus bodies must be finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi proves that everything finite must be originated. Your body is finite—you know it will end. Are you living every day as if you are created and accountable to the Creator who brought you into existence, or do you ignore this truth and live as if you are autonomous and eternal? What practical change would it take for you to fully submit to your finitude?
?
The argument shows that assuming infinite leads to contradiction. In your own life, do you assume something is infinite (your health, your youth, your opportunities) and that assumption leads to spiritual neglect? What specific finite gift from Allah are you taking for granted right now, and how will you thank Him for it today?

If Razi's logical necessity of finitude is undeniable, why do you still act as if your time will never run out? What would it mean for you to truly believe that your life is finite and that every moment is a trust from Allah? The Second Premise: Finite Things Require a Choosing Agent
Razi proves that finitude implies need for a specifier, thus bodies are created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that everything finite in magnitude must be originated (created), because its specific size requires a choosing agent to avoid the impossibility of preference without a reason.

Core Idea
Every finite thing needs a specifier; without it, preference without a reason is impossible.

Razi builds his case for the creation of the universe step by step. Having shown that bodies are finite in size, he now argues that finitude itself forces us to acknowledge a creator. The proof is subtle but powerful: it uses the principle that equal possibilities cannot resolve without an external chooser.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and the translation side by side. Pay attention to the logical structure: the premise, the proof, and the conclusion.
Notice that Razi uses a conditional chain (إذا... إلا...) to argue.
2
Identify the three magnitudes
The argument mentions three magnitudes: the actual size, a size larger by a seed, and a size smaller by a seed. Understand why these three are equally possible in the intellect.
'Seed' (ذرة) is a minimal unit, like an atom.
3
Understand the need for a specifier
Ask yourself: why can't one of these magnitudes simply be actual without a cause? Razi says that would be 'preponderance without a preponderator', which is rationally impossible. Connect this to the principle of sufficient reason.
This step is crucial for grasping the kalam argument for God's existence.
4
Trace the conclusion
Razi links this to the earlier proof: bodies are finite, finitude implies need for a free agent, and anything from a free agent is originated. So bodies are originated. Walk through each step of the chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثانية وهي في بيان أن كل ما كان متناهيا في المقدار فهو محدث . والدليل عليه أن كل ما كان متناهيا في المقدار فإنه لا يمتنع في العقل فرض وجوده أزيد من ذلك المقدار بذرة أو أنقص منه بذرة، والعلم بجواز ذلك ضروري، وإذا استوت المقادير الثلاثة أعني حصول ذلك المقدار وحصول ذلك الأزيد منه وحصول الأنقص منه استحال رجحان ذلك القدر على ما أزيد منه أو أنقص منه، إلا بتخصيص مخصص، وإيجاد قادر، وإلا فقد ترجح أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر من غير مرجح، وهو محال. وقد بينا في البرهان الأول : أن كل ما كان فعلا لفاعل مختار فهو محدث فإذن قد ثبت أن كل جسم فهو متناه في المقدار، وثبت أن كل ما كان متناهيا في المقدار فهو محتاج في وجوده إلى الفاعل المختار وثبت أن كل ما كان محتاجًا في وجوده إلى الفاعل المختار فهو محدث فيلزم الجزم بأن كل جسم محدث.
As for the second premise, which is on demonstrating that everything finite in magnitude is originated. The proof for it is that everything finite in magnitude, it is not impossible in the intellect to assume its existence greater than that magnitude by a seed or less than it by a seed, and knowledge of its possibility is necessary. When the three magnitudes are equal, namely the attainment of that magnitude, the attainment of what is greater than it, and the attainment of what is less than it, it is impossible for that specific magnitude to be preferred over what is greater than it or less than it, except through the specification of a specifier and the bringing into existence of a powerful agent. Otherwise, one of the two possibilities would be preferred over the other without a preferrer, which is impossible. And we have explained in the first proof that everything that is an act of a free agent is originated. Therefore, it has been established that every body is finite in magnitude, and it has been established that everything finite in magnitude is in need in its existence of a free agent, and it has been established that everything in need in its existence of a free agent is originated, so the certainty follows that every body is originated.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the contingency and temporal origin (huduth) of bodies. It serves as the 'second premise' (المقدمة الثانية) following the proofs that bodies are finite. The argument: For any finite magnitude, the intellect can conceive it being larger or smaller by a trifling amount; this possibility is known necessarily. Now the actual magnitude, the larger, and the smaller are all equally possible (none inherently necessary). Without an external specifying agent (mukhasșis) that brings one particular size into existence, it would be a case of preponderance without a preponderator (tarjih bila murajjih), which is rationally impossible. Thus, a finite body must have a free agent (fa'il mukhtar) that gives it its specific size. Since anything that is the act of a free agent is originated (muhdath), it follows that every finite body is originated. The conclusion of the whole argument is then restated: bodies are finite (from previous proofs), finite things need a free agent, and everything needing a free agent is originated, so bodies are originated.

Key Terms
متناه في المقدار
محدث
تخصيص مخصص
فاعل مختار
ترجيح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A finite object could simply exist eternally without any cause for its specific size.
Correction: Razi shows that among equally possible sizes, one cannot be chosen without a specifier, so a finite thing must have a cause that specifies its size; this cause is a free agent, and anything caused by a free agent is originated, so it cannot be eternal.

Checkpoint
Why must a finite magnitude have a specifying agent according to Razi?
A) Because larger and smaller magnitudes are impossible.
B) Because the actual magnitude is just one among equally possible sizes, so a choice is needed.
C) Because finite things are always composite.
D) Because every effect needs a cause.

Key Takeaway
Finite magnitude alone proves that the world needs a creator, because only a free agent can choose one specific size among equally possible alternatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your own body is finite in every detail—its height, shape, and even the number of cells. If every specific size requires a choosing agent, what does that mean for how you view your existence? Are you living with the daily awareness that your very form is chosen by Allah, or do you take it for granted? This week, before you go to sleep, reflect on one specific aspect of your physical existence and say 'Alhamdulillah' with the certainty that it was deliberately given to you.
?
The proof shows that without a specifier, preference would occur without reason—a logical impossibility. In your own life, do you sometimes act as if events are random or meaningless? Where do you need to remind yourself that nothing in your life happens without the specific will of Allah? Commit today to recite 'Qaddarallahu wa ma shaa fa'ala' (Allah decreed and what He wills He does) when you face something you don't understand.
?
If the three magnitudes are equally possible, then the actual magnitude you have is not necessary but a gift. When you look at your own capabilities, wealth, or status, do you see them as inherent to you or as contingent specifications chosen for you by the Creator? How would your humility and gratitude change if you truly internalised that your current state is just one of many possibilities that Allah chose for you? Write down one thing you're proud of and remind yourself: 'This could have been otherwise, but Allah chose it for me.'

Razi's Third Proof: If Body Eternal, It Cannot Move
Razi begins the third proof with two conditional statements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third proof for the temporality of bodies, which argues that an eternal body cannot change its location.

Core Idea
An eternal body would be frozen in one place forever.

This lesson presents the beginning of Razi's third proof for the temporality of bodies. Although the passage is incomplete, it establishes a key premise: if a body were eternal, it would be fixed in a specific location for all eternity, making movement impossible. This sets the stage for concluding that bodies are not eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: Razi states the proof title, then a two-part conditional statement.
The passage is short but dense. Read it twice.
2
Identify the two conditionals
The proof consists of two 'if...then' statements. First: if a body were eternal, it would be in a specific location in pre-eternity. Second: if it were in that location in pre-eternity, it could not leave.
These conditionals form the logical core of the proof.
3
Consider the missing conclusion
Razi does not state the conclusion here, but it is implied: since bodies do move (change location), they cannot be eternal. Think about how the conditionals lead to this conclusion.
The full argument would continue on the next page.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثالث في حدوث الأجسام : أن الجسم لو كان أزليا لكان في الأزل حاصلاً في حيز معين، ولو كان في الأزل حاصلا في حيز معين لامتنع خروجه عنه
The third proof on the temporality of bodies: That if a body were eternal, then in pre-eternity it would be situated in a specific location, and if it were in pre-eternity situated in a specific location, its departure from it would be impossible.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage begins the third proof (البرهان الثالث) for the temporality of bodies. Razi presents the premise: if a body were eternal (azali), it would have to be in a specific place from pre-eternity. The second conditional: if it were in a specific place from pre-eternity, it would be impossible for it to leave that place. The argument is incomplete in this window; it likely continues to conclude that since bodies do move, they cannot be eternal. This proof is a simple argument from the impossibility of an eternal body changing its location.

Key Terms
أزلي
جسم
حيز
خروج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An eternal body could still move or change location.
Correction: Razi argues that an eternal body would be fixed in its original location for eternity, making any departure impossible.

Checkpoint
What does Razi say would be the consequence if a body were eternal?
A) It would be impossible for it to leave its location.
B) It would be able to move freely.
C) It would have no location.
D) It would be composed of parts.

Key Takeaway
Razi's third proof argues that an eternal body cannot change location, implying that observed movement proves bodies are temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you find yourself attached to a place, a habit, or a relationship as if it were permanent, what does this proof teach you about the futility of clinging to anything besides Allah? Is there something in your life you have made 'eternal' in your heart that is not? What step can you take today to break that attachment?
?
If an eternal body cannot change, then only Allah is truly eternal and unchanging. In what areas of your life have you tried to find stability or security in changing things—your health, wealth, or reputation? How does this proof challenge you to redirect your dependence entirely to Allah? What will you commit to changing today?

Fourth Proof: Only One Necessary Being
Razi proves the impossibility of two necessary beings via composition argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that only one necessary being can exist, and that everything else is possible and therefore temporal.

Core Idea
Multiple necessary beings are impossible because each would be composite and dependent.

Razi presents a precise reductio argument to establish that only one being is necessary. He then begins to show that whatever is possible must be temporal. This lesson walks through the logical steps and their implications, focusing on the composition argument against multiple necessary beings.

Guided Path
1
Follow the reductio structure
Start by reading the passage and the translation. Notice how Razi sets up a conditional: 'If we suppose two necessary existents, then each would be composite, hence possible, contradicting necessity.' Trace each step of this logical chain.
The proof relies on the principle that sharing and differing aspects make a thing composite.
2
Understand composition and dependence
Razi argues that any two necessary beings would share 'essential necessity' but differ in 'individuation'. Since the sharer and differencer are distinct, each being is composed of two aspects. A composite depends on its parts, so it cannot be necessary—it is possible in itself.
This is the key insight: necessity precludes any composition or dependence.
3
Recognize the conclusion
The conclusion is that only one necessary existent is possible. Everything else, lacking this necessity, is possible in itself. The second premise (incomplete in the text) would then show that every possible is temporal.
The text cuts off mid-proof, but the first premise is fully established.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Note that this argument does not deny that God has attributes (like knowledge, power). It only rules out two distinct necessary beings. God's attributes are not separate existents but aspects of the one necessary essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الرابع في حدوث ما سوی الله تعالى : فنقول : كل ما سوى الواجب الوجود الواحد فإنه ممكن لذاته، وكل ممكن لذاته فهو محدث ،فإذن كل ما سوى الموجود الواحد فإنه محدث.
بيان المقدمة الأولى وهي قولنا : كل ما سوى الموجود الواحد فهو ممكن لذاته، فنقول : الدليل عليه أنا لو فرضنا موجودين يكون كل واحد منهما واجبًا لذاته فلا بد وأن يكونا متشاركين في الوجوب الذاتي، ولا بد وأن يكونا غير متشاركين في التعيين، وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز، فإذن يكون كل واحد منهما مركبا من الوجود الذاتي الذي حصلت به المشاركة ومن التعيين الذي به حصلت المباينة لكن كل مركب فإنه مفتقر في ماهيته وفي تحققه إلى كل واحد من مفرديه، وكل واحد من مفرديه مغاير له ؛ لأن الكل مغاير لكل واحد من أجزائه، فإذن كل مركب فإنه مفتقر في تحققه إلى غيره وكل مفتقر في تحققه إلى غيره فهو ممكن لذاته، فإذن كل مركب فهو ممكن لذاته فإذن لو فرضنا موجودين واجبي الوجود لكان كل واحد منهما ممكنا لذاته والثاني محال فإذن فرض موجودين واجبي الوجود يجب أن يكون محالاً، فثبت أن كل ما سوى الموجود الواحد يجب أن يكون ممكنا لذاته. وأما المقدمة الثانية وهي أن كل ما كان ممكنا لذاته فإنه يجب أن يكون محدثاً، فالبرهان على صحته أن كل ما كان ممكنا لذاته فإنه مفتقر في رجحان وجوده
The fourth proof on the temporality of everything other than God: We say: Everything other than the one Necessary Existence is possible in itself, and everything possible in itself is temporal (hadith). Therefore everything other than the one Existent is temporal. Explanation of the first premise, which is our saying: Everything other than the one Existent is possible in itself. We say: The proof of it is that if we suppose two existents each of which is necessary in itself, then they must share in essential necessity and must differ in individuation. That by which they share is other than that by which they differ. Thus each of them is composed of the essential existence by which participation occurs and of the individuation by which distinction occurs. But every composite is dependent in its quiddity (mahiyya) and in its realization on each of its components. And each of its components is other than it, because the whole is other than each of its parts. Therefore, every composite is dependent in its realization on something other than itself, and everything dependent in its realization on something other than itself is possible in itself. Hence every composite is possible in itself. Consequently, if we suppose two necessary existents, each of them would be possible in itself, which is contradictory. Therefore the supposition of two necessary existents must be impossible. So it is established that everything other than the one Existent must be possible in itself. As for the second premise, that everything possible in itself must be temporal, the proof of its soundness is that everything possible in itself is dependent for the preponderance of its existence...
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof that everything other than God is temporal. It first states the conclusion: all besides the One Necessary Being is possible and thus temporal. The proof of the first premise uses a reductio: assume two necessary existents. They would share the attribute of being necessary by essence but differ in identity. This common and distinctive aspects make each composite. A composite depends on its parts and thus on something other than itself, making it possible in itself—contradicting its necessity. Therefore, only one necessary being exists, and all else is possible. The second premise, that every possible is temporal, is begun but the text cuts off; the argument would involve a preponderator for existence over non-existence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this proof denies that God has multiple attributes (like knowledge and power), arguing that would make Him composite.
Correction: The proof only denies multiple distinct necessary beings, not internal attributes. God's attributes are not separate existents but aspects of one simple essence.

Checkpoint
Why cannot there be two distinct necessary beings, according to this proof?
A) They would be too powerful
B) They would share in necessity but differ in identity, making each composite and thus dependent
C) They would have no common ground
D) They would not be eternal

Key Takeaway
The oneness of the necessary being is established by showing that multiplicity would entail composition and dependence, which are incompatible with necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you ever act as though there are multiple ultimate authorities or sources of meaning? How does this proof challenge you to see all other things as dependent and temporal?
?
If every composite thing is dependent and possible, what does that say about your own self-sufficiency? Where do you rely on something other than God as if it were necessary?
?
When you feel torn between multiple loyalties—work, family, desires—ask yourself: are these loyalties like the two supposed necessary beings? Can any of them be truly self-sufficient? This proof shows only One. Will you commit to aligning your heart to that One alone?

The Third Proof: Bodies Cannot Be Eternal
Imam Razi completes the third proof of bodily origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses a proof by contradiction to demonstrate that bodies cannot be eternal, based on the impossibility of motion.

Core Idea
Eternal bodies would be motionless; motion exists, so bodies are not eternal.

Imam Razi presents his third proof for the temporal origination of bodies. This proof is a logical argument that shows the impossibility of bodies being eternal. By examining the implications of eternity for motion, Razi demonstrates that the observed possibility of motion contradicts the nature of an eternal being. Let's follow the steps of this elegant reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the overall argument
Read the passage once to grasp the big picture: Razi argues that if bodies were eternal, motion would be impossible. Since motion is possible (we observe it), bodies cannot be eternal.
This is a proof by contradiction (burhān al-khalf).
2
Examine the key premise about location
The first premise states that an eternal body would occupy a specific location from eternity. This is considered self-evident because any spatial object must be somewhere. Understand why this premise is necessary for the proof.
The term 'self-evident' (badīhiyya) indicates the premise is immediately known.
3
Connect to impossibility of motion
If the body is eternally fixed in one location, it cannot leave that location (since the eternal does not cease). Therefore, motion is impossible. Then Razi points out that motion is not impossible; we see bodies move. Hence, the original assumption (body is eternal) must be false.
For unobserved bodies, he refers to an earlier proof establishing the possibility of motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكان يلزم أن يمتنع على كل واحد من أفراد الأجسام كونه متحركاً، ولما كان هذا الثاني باطلا كان القول بكون الجسم أزليا باطلا. أما المقدمة الأولى وهي قولنا : إن الجسم لو كان أزليا لكان في الأزل حاصلا في حيز معين فهي مقدمة بديهية؛ لأنا قد ذكرنا أن المراد من المتحيز ما يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة حسية بأنه هنا أو هناك، وكل موجود هذا شأنه وصفته فإنه لا بد وأن يكون مختصا بجهة معينة ومكان معين، فثبت أن الجسم لو كان أزليا لكان في الأزل حاصلا في حيز معين ولو كان كذلك لكان حصوله في ذلك الحيز المعين أزليّا وقد بينا في البرهان الأول أن الأزلي لا يزول، فكان يمتنع عقلا زوال ذلك الحصول، وإذا امتنع زواله امتنعت الحركة عليه، فثبت أن الجسم لو كان أزليا لكانت الحركة ممتنعة عليه، لكن الحركة غير ممتنعة عليه، أما في الأجسام المشاهدة فالحس دال على جواز الحركة عليها، وأما في الأجسام الغائبة فالبرهان عليه هو الذي ذكرناه في البرهان الأول، فظهر أن الجسم يمتنع أن يكون أزليا.
It would then be necessary that motion be impossible for every individual body. Since this [impossibility of motion] is false, the statement that the body is eternal is false. As for the first premise, which is our statement: 'If the body were eternal, it would from eternity be situated in a specific location' – it is a self-evident premise; because we have mentioned that what is meant by 'occupying space' is that which can be pointed to by sensory indication as being here or there, and every existent that has this quality and description must necessarily be specific to a particular direction and particular place. So it is established that if the body were eternal, it would from eternity be situated in a specific location, and if that were so, its being in that specific location would be eternal. We have shown in the first proof that the eternal does not cease, so the cessation of that [location] would be rationally impossible. If its cessation is impossible, then motion upon it is impossible. Thus it is established that if the body were eternal, motion would be impossible for it. But motion is not impossible for it – as for observed bodies, sense perception indicates the possibility of motion upon them, and as for unobserved bodies, the proof for it is that which we mentioned in the first proof. Thus it becomes evident that the body cannot be eternal.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of bodies. It is a proof by contradiction (burhān al-khalf). The author first states the conclusion: if bodies were eternal, motion would be impossible; since motion is possible, they are not eternal. He then unpacks the key premise: an eternal body would have to occupy a specific location from eternity, and that location would be eternal and hence unchangeable. Since the eternal cannot cease, the body could never leave that location, making motion impossible. The premise is self-evident because any spatial object is necessarily located somewhere. The author then appeals to empirical observation: we see bodies moving, so motion is possible. For unobserved bodies, he refers to an earlier proof (al-burhān al-awwal) that establishes the possibility of motion in principle. Therefore, the assumption of eternity leads to a contradiction, so bodies must be originated (muḥdath).

Key Terms
أزلي
حيز
متحرك
بديهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes that all bodies actually move, so if a body is stationary, it could be eternal.
Correction: The proof only requires that motion be possible for a body, not actual. Even a stationary body can potentially move; eternity would make motion impossible, not just absent.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, what would be impossible if a body were eternal?
A) It would be composite.
B) It would be originated.
C) It would be subject to motion.
D) It would be in a location.

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that if bodies were eternal, motion would be impossible; since motion is possible, bodies must be originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that every body in existence is originated and cannot be eternal. Is there any physical object in your life that you treat as if it will last forever—perhaps your wealth, your health, or your youth? What would it mean for your heart to fully release that false attachment today?
?
If motion were impossible for eternal things, and you have a soul that can change, grow, and repent, then your soul is clearly not eternal—it is created and dependent on its Creator. When did you last truly move your soul toward Him through sincere repentance? If you have been spiritually stagnant, what one change can you commit to starting tomorrow?
?
The proof shows that the universe cannot be self-sufficient because it is made of bodies that are originated. Do your daily actions reflect a deep conviction that you and everything you own are contingent upon God at every moment? Or do you sometimes feel self-sufficient? How can you intentionally remind yourself of your dependence today?

The First Premise: Only One Necessary Existent Can Exist
Imam Razi proves the first premise of the fourth proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why only one necessary existent can exist, and therefore everything else must be possible in itself.

Core Idea
If two necessary existents existed, each would be composite and thus impossible.

Imam Razi begins the fourth proof by establishing that everything other than the One Necessary Existent is possible in itself. This is not obvious; it rests on a powerful argument that the supposition of two necessary existents leads to contradiction. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the premise
The passage states the first premise of the proof: everything other than the One Necessary Existent is possible in itself. Read carefully to see how Razi defends it.
2
Follow the argument: what happens if we suppose two necessary existents?
Razi uses a proof by contradiction. Suppose two necessary existents. They must share in necessity and differ in individuation. This leads to composition.
Composition means each would be made of parts.
3
Understand why composition implies possibility
Every composite depends on its parts, and dependence means it is possible in itself. Thus both would be possible, contradicting necessity. So only one necessary existent can exist.
This concludes that everything else is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الرابع في حدوث ما سوی الله تعالى : فنقول : كل ما سوى الواجب الوجود الواحد فإنه ممكن لذاته، وكل ممكن لذاته فهو محدث، فإذن كل ما سوى الموجود الواحد فإنه محدث. بيان المقدمة الأولى وهي قولنا : كل ما سوى الموجود الواحد فهو ممكن لذاته، فنقول : الدليل عليه أنا لو فرضنا موجودين يكون كل واحد منهما واجبًا لذاته فلا بد وأن يكونا متشاركين في الوجوب الذاتي، ولا بد وأن يكونا غير متشاركين في التعيين، وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز، فإذن يكون كل واحد منهما مركبا من الوجود الذاتي الذي حصلت به المشاركة ومن التعيين الذي به حصلت المباينة لكن كل مركب فإنه مفتقر في ماهيته وفي تحققه إلى كل واحد من مفرديه، وكل واحد من مفرديه مغاير له ؛ لأن الكل مغاير لكل واحد من أجزائه، فإذن كل مركب فإنه مفتقر في تحققه إلى غيره وكل مفتقر في تحققه إلى غيره فهو ممكن لذاته، فإذن كل مركب فهو ممكن لذاته فإذن لو فرضنا موجودين واجبي الوجود لكان كل واحد منهما ممكنا لذاته والثاني محال فإذن فرض موجودين واجبي الوجود يجب أن يكون محالاً، فثبت أن كل ما سوى الموجود الواحد يجب أن يكون ممكنا لذاته. وأما المقدمة الثانية وهي أن كل ما كان ممكنا لذاته فإنه يجب أن يكون محدثاً، فالبرهان على صحته أن كل ما كان ممكنا لذاته فإنه مفتقر في رجحان وجوده على عدمه إلى المؤثر، وكل ما كان مفتقرًا في وجوده إلى المؤثر فإنه يجب أن يكون محدثًا. بيان المقدمة الأولى: أن الممكن ما تكون ماهيته قابلة للعدم والوجود على البدل، وما كان كذلك امتنع رجحان أحد الطرفين على الآخر إلا لمؤثر منفصل. بيان المقدمة الثانية : أن نقول : الافتقار إلى المؤثر إما أن يحصل حال الوجود أو حال العدم، فإن حصل حال الوجود فإما أن يحصل حال البقاء أو محال الحدوث، ولا جائز أن يحصل حال البقاء، وإلا لزم أن يكون الشيء حال بقائه مفتقرًا إلى موجد يوجده ، وإلى مكون يكونه، وذلك محال ؛ لأن إيجاد الموجود تحصيل حاصل، وتحصيل الحاصل محال في بداهة العقول فلم يبق إلا أن يكون افتقار الأثر إلى المؤثر إما حاصلا حال الحدوث أو حال العدم، وعلى التقديرين فإنه يلزم أن يكون كل مفتقر في وجوده إلى المؤثر فإنه يكون محدثاً، فثبت أن كل ما سوى الواحد ممكن لذاته وثبت أن كل ما كان ممكنا لذاته فهو مفتقر في وجوده إلى المؤثر، وثبت أن كل ما كان مفتقرًا في وجوده إلى المؤثر فإذن يلزم من هذا البرهان أن كل ما سوى الواحد لا بد وأن يكون محدثًا . وهذا البرهان يفيد حدوث الأجسام والأعراض، والعقول والنفوس والهيولى، ويفيد أن واجب الوجود واحد وهو جل جلاله وبالجملة فهو برهان عظيم وافٍ بإثبات أكثر المباحث الشريفة الإلهية.
The fourth proof concerning the origination of what is other than God, the Exalted: We say: Everything other than the One Necessary Existent is possible in itself, and everything possible in itself is originated. Therefore, everything other than the One Existent is originated. Explanation of the first premise, which is our statement: 'Everything other than the One Existent is possible in itself.' We say: The proof for it is that if we suppose two existents each necessary in themselves, they must share in essential necessity and must differ in individuation. That by which they share is other than that by which they differ. Thus each would be composed of the essential existence by which sharing occurs and the individuation by which distinction occurs. But every composite is dependent in its quiddity and realization on each of its parts, and each of its parts is other than it because the whole is other than each of its parts. Therefore every composite is dependent in its realization on something other than itself, and everything dependent in its realization on something other than itself is possible in itself. Thus every composite is possible in itself. Therefore, if we suppose two existents necessary in themselves, each would be possible in itself, which is impossible. Hence the supposition of two necessary existents must be impossible. Thus it is established that everything other than the One Existent must be possible in itself. As for the second premise, that everything possible in itself must be originated: the proof of its correctness is that everything possible in itself is dependent for the preponderance of its existence over its non-existence on an effecting cause, and everything dependent in its existence on an effecting cause must be originated. Explanation of the first [sub-]premise: The possible is that whose quiddity is receptive of non-existence and existence alternately, and when that is so, the preponderance of one side over the other is impossible except through a separate effecting cause. Explanation of the second [sub-]premise: We say: Dependence on the effecting cause occurs either in the state of existence or in the state of non-existence. If it occurs in the state of existence, then either in the state of continuance or at the moment of origination. It cannot occur in the state of continuance, otherwise it would require that a thing in its state of continuance be dependent on an originator to originate it and a maker to make it, which is impossible because bringing the existent into existence is a reactualization of what already exists, and reactualization of what already exists is impossible in the immediate judgment of intellects. Thus nothing remains except that the dependence of the effect on the effecting cause occurs either at the moment of origination or in the state of non-existence. On either assumption, it follows that everything dependent in its existence on an effecting cause is originated. So it is established that everything other than the One is possible in itself, and it is established that everything possible in itself is dependent in its existence on an effecting cause, and it is established that everything dependent in its existence on an effecting cause is originated. Therefore it follows from this proof that everything other than the One must be originated. And this proof yields the origination of bodies, accidents, intellects, souls, and prime matter, and it yields that the Necessary Existent is One – He is majestic is His glory – and in summary it is a great proof sufficient for establishing most of the noble divine investigations.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a self-contained proof (burhān) for the existence of the One Necessary Existent and the origination of all else. It proceeds in two premises. First premise: Everything other than the One is possible in itself. The author demonstrates this by showing that if there were two necessary existents, they would share necessity and differ in individuality, making each composite. Since composition entails dependence and dependence entails possibility, both would be possible, contradicting necessity. Therefore only one necessary existent can exist, and everything else is possible. Second premise: Everything possible is originated (muḥdath). The argumentation here is twofold. The possible, by definition, is equally receptive to existence and non-existence, so it needs an external cause (muʼaththir) to tip the balance. This dependence must occur either at the moment of origination or in the state of non-existence (since dependence during continuance is impossible – that would be re-creating what already exists, which is absurd). Thus the possible is originated. The conclusion: everything other than the One is originated. The author then notes the wide scope of this proof: it applies to bodies, accidents, intellects, souls, and prime matter, establishing both the unity of God and the origination of the world.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود (Necessary Existent)
ممكن لذاته (possible in itself)
مركب (composite)
تعيين (individuation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the proof show about the supposition of two necessary existents?
A) It is possible.
B) It leads to each being composite and thus possible, which is a contradiction.
C) It proves they can coexist.
D) It shows they are both necessary.

Key Takeaway
Only one necessary existent can exist, therefore all other things are possible in themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After reading this proof, do you find yourself truly convinced that there can only be one God, or do you still harbor some doubt in your heart? What would it take to remove that doubt?
?
This argument shows that everything other than God is possible and dependent. How does that realization affect your daily reliance on created things versus your reliance on the Creator?
?
If the existence of more than one necessary being is logically impossible, what does that tell you about the exclusivity of worship and the seriousness of shirk? Have you ever committed an act of shirk in your heart by attributing ultimate power or control to anyone other than Allah?
